Preface 
This paper is the final part of the author’s degree within ‘Master of Architectural Engineering’ at the Technical 
University of Denmark. The thesis is formed from a self-formulated goal for the project and from an initial 
project request by HusKoncept. During the creation of this thesis, the project has developed to become more 
independent from its initial assignment at HusKoncept, and thereby implements a broader assessment, 
considering both Australian and Danish industry.  
Conclusions drawn in this project are based on the personal studies of results and literature, along with 
weighted normalisation; hence they are not fitted for public disclosure.  
The project has been written as a collaborative thesis between the Technical University of Denmark, 
University of New South Wales and with external partners at HusKoncept.

providing data and knowledge for the case studies and methodology during the project. For providing 
excellent and tireless guidance along with valuable discussions throughout the entire project, I would like to 
thank my two main supervisors, Morten Birkved and Philip Oldfield

==PAGE:4==
 
II 
Abstract 
 
The focus on sustainability and the earth’s wellbeing has been increasing over recent decades and has now 
spread to most industries. The environmental impacts caused by humans have been found to be significantly 
driven by the Built Environment. When assessing the sustainability of a building, it is mostly checked against 
similar buildings, often within the same country. The responsibility of our impact is, however, a global 
responsibility. So how can our buildings be called ‘sustainable’ when their assessment does not relate to the 
actual environmental means of our world? And how can we make countries ‘compete’ in optimising their 
impact share from the built environment, when we compare using different measures?  
This project thus aims to assess buildings’ environmental sustainability, suggesting a probable more 
sustainable solution and assessing the impact in absolute terms, considering the planetary boundaries, and 
both Danish and Australian cases. 
 
The study applies normalisation references, developed for defined impact categories, on resulting impacts 
from an LCA of the various case studies. The normalisation references are allocated to the dwelling by 
considering what is a ‘fair share’ of the available carrying capacity, based on an economic household spending 
assessment. The applied characterisation methodology is ILCD and the modelling software used was 
openLCA. 
 
The case studies represent traditional building designs for single-family dwellings in Denmark and Australia, 
comparing the sustainability and impact contributors from the two countries. As a suggestion for a more 
sustainable solution, a design proposal for a terraced modular housing design was also assessed. The design 
originated in Denmark, but a modified version for Australia was also developed. 
 
While the assessments showed that none of the four case studies could be deemed absolute sustainable, 
thus complying with the allocated carrying capacity of the earth, the modular designs were found to be, 
relatively measured, more sustainable than the prevalent building designs. 
 
From the normalisation references developed in the literature, 7 impact categories were assessed: climate 
change (temperature increase and radiative forcing thresholds), freshwater ecotoxicity, freshwater and 
marine eutrophication, ozone depletion, and photochemical ozone formation. The Danish case studies (both 
prevalent and modular) exceeded the limits in 5 of the 7 categories, the Danish reference study exceeding 
with a factor 3 to 40 and the Danish modular study in an exceedance range from 2-28. The Australian cases 
exceeded all categories but ‘ozone depletion’, with an exceedance range between 22-570 for the Australian 
reference scenario and 18-281 for the Australian modular scenario. For all cases climate change presented 
the largest exceedance. 
 
Through the project it was further concluded that uncertainties, caused by poor availability of local data and 
non-extensively developed allocation methodologies, may have skewed the interpretations of the final 
result.  
 
Conclusions drawn in this project are based on the personal studies of results and literature, along with 
weighted normalisation; hence they are not fit for public disclosure.  
 
 
==PAGE:5==
 
III 
Resumé 
 
Der bliver i dag fokuseret mere og mere på bæredygtighed og jordens velbefindende. Denne bekymring og 
fokus har nu spredt sig til de fleste industrier. En stor andel af de menneskeskabte, miljømæssige, 
påvirkninger er fundet til at være grundet af bygningsindustrien. Dette projekt bestræber sig derfor på at 
undersøge bygningers miljømæssige bæredygtighed, undersøge en mulig mere bæredygtig byggeløsning og 
vurdere de miljømæssige påvirkninger i et absolut perspektiv, der tilgodeser bæreevnen af og de tilgængelig 
ressourcer på jorden. 
 
Projektet anvender normaliseringsreferencer, der er udviklet for prædefinerede påvirkningskategorier, og 
normaliserer hermed de resulterende miljøpåvirkninger fundet ved livscyklus-analyser af forskellige case 
studier. Normaliseringsreferencerne er allokeret i forhold til hvad der anses som en ’fair share’ af den 
tilgængelige kapacitet. Denne allokering er baseret på en økonomisk husholdningsforbrugs statistikker 
fordeling. Den anvendte karakteriseringsmetode er ILCD, og modelleringen af LCA’erne er foretaget i 
openLCA. 
 
Casene undersøgt i dette studie repræsenterer traditionelle byggemetoder for et enfamilieshus i hhv. 
Danmark og Australien, der muliggør en sammenligning af den miljømæssige bæredygtighed og hvilke 
faktorer der påvirker denne, på tværs af de to lande. Endvidere analyseres der en potentiel optimerende 
løsning, resulterende i en lavere miljøpåvirkning. Dette forslag er baseret på et design af et modulært 
rækkehus, udviklet i Danmark. For at kunne analysere scenarier i Australien, er dette design tilpasset til at 
overholde minimum lovgivningen i Australien.  
 
Det overordnede resultat af studiet viste at ingen af de analyserede case-studier kunne defineres som 
bæredygtige i et absolut perspektiv, da de ikke holdt sig inden for grænseværdierne defineret for jordens 
kapacitet.  En relativ sammenligning viste dog, at det modulære design performede bedre end de 
traditionelle byggemetoder, og de blev derfor fundet, relativt, mere bæredygtige.   
 
Ud af de udviklede normaliseringsreferencer fundet i litteraturen, blev 7 af påvirkningskategorierne brugt i 
projektet: climate change (temperaturstigning og grænseværdier for radiativ stråling), freshwater 
ecotoxicity, freshwater and marine eutrophication, ozone depletion and photochemical ozone formation. De 
danske cases (både traditionelle og modulære) blev fundet til at overskride 5 ud af de 7 kategorier med en 
faktor 3 til 40 for the Danske reference studie og 2-28 for det danske modulære design. De Australske cases 
overskred 6 - alle undtagen ’ozone depletion’, med en faktor 22-570 for the australske reference studie og 
mellem 18-281 for the australske modulære design. For alle studier var kategorien ’climate change’ dem der 
overskred med de største værdier. 
 
Undervejs i projektet blev det endvidere konkluderet at usikkerhederne medført af begrænset 
tilgængelighed af lokal/regional data og de begrænset udviklede allokeringsmetoder, kan have påvirket 
fortolkningen af de endelige resultater i et uhensigtsmæssigt omfang. 
 
Konklusionerne i dette projekt er baseret på studiet af resultater og litteratur af forfatteren alene samt 
baseret på vægtede og normaliserede resultater. Projektets konklusioner er derfor ikke egnet til 
offentliggørelse og beslutningstagen på baggrund heraf.

REFERENCE 
Absolute 
Sustainability 
The environmental impacts of a product/system held against the 
carrying capacity/limiting threshold of impact or resource usage. 
Resulting in an ‘absolute’ value, determining whether the product 
assessment exceeds the limit of allocated emissions allocated to that 
product. 
 
Carrying 
Capacity 
Threshold/limit of a region, country or Earth, which can support the 
number of people, animals, or crops within/upon it without 
environmental degradation. 
 
Environmental 
impact 
A consequence of an environmental intervention in the 
environmental system  
 
Impact category Class representing environmental issues of concern E.g. Climate 
change, Acidification, Ecotoxicity etc. 
(ISO 14044 
2006) 
Person 
equivalent 
“A person equivalent is, in this context, defined as the maximum 
amount of environmental impact that an average person can cause 
when society as a whole must stay within the safe operating space.”  
(Bjørn & 
Hauschild 
2015b)

boundaries 
“The space within which humanity can safely operate without 
interfering with the system of the earth and the biophysical processes 
and sub systems of the planet” 
(Rockström et 
al. 2009)

analysis

regarding methods and data on the outcome of the study  
(ISO 14044 
2006)

sustainability

Introduction 
 
Page 1 of 85 
1. Introduction 
The focus on sustainability and the earth’s wellbeing has been increasing over recent decades and has now 
spread to most industries. The ‘Brundtland Report’ is perhaps the most oft-cited definition of ‘sustainable 
development’ (UNECE n.d.), outlining the term as such: 
 
“Humanity has the ability to make development sustainable to ensure that it meets the needs of the 
present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs. The concept of 
sustainable development does imply limits - not absolute limits but limitations imposed by the present state 
of technology and social organization on environmental resources and by the ability of the biosphere to 
absorb the effects of human activities.” (Brundtland-Commission 1987) 
 
It is widely accepted that climate and ecosystem changes are caused by humanity, and the IPCC have 
proclaimed to be 95% sure that the main reason for global warming is human activities (IPCC 2014). It is still 
a topic being investigated, as no one can accurately predict the future consequences, but scientists proclaim 
it to be the cause of weather changes, loss of biodiversity, and rising sea levels, amongst others (UNEP 2009; 
IPCC 2014). It is agreed upon by most experts that the earth will be impacted by dangerous climate changes 
which will have dramatic implications on economy, society and environment of the world (UNEP 2009). 
 
Recent research concludes that the level of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere are now reaching the 
highest level for 800,000 years. UN-researchers are urging action, as observations made have never shown 
such sudden increases in the level of CO2 in the atmosphere, see Figure 1 (Møller 2017; WMO 2017). 
 
 
FIGURE 1 – CO2 EMISSION INCREASES. COMPARING RECENT INCREASES TO SECOND MOST SUDDEN INCREASE, 12,000-16,000 YEARS 
AGO. AS ILLUSTRATED IN (WMO 2017) 
 
Research is consistent in suggesting that should levels of CO2 in the atmosphere continue to increase, then 
catastrophic weather events such as droughts, storms, extinctions, etc. will become more commonplace, 
impacting all life on earth (Møller 2017; WMO 2017; Jacobs 2017; Khanna 2016; Corradi & Lajoie n.d.). 
 
According to (UN Environment and International Energy Agency 2017), the built environment is said to be 
responsible for 39% of the total energy consumption globally. Hereof, 28% originates from energy 
consumption of building, and 11% from the construction industry, hence embodied energy.  
==PAGE:12==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 2 of 85 
However, it is also said to be the industry with the greatest potential to reduce GHG-emissions, while being 
cost-efficient, compared to other high-emission industries. Policies, knowledge and technology to obtain 
these reductions already exist and are proven to work (UNEP 2009). 
 
Buildings contribute to environmental degradation throughout their entire lifetime, from the extraction of 
raw materials to their operation, to the demolition and waste treatment of the building at the end of its life. 
All these ’processes’ require energy and emit Greenhouse gases (GHG’s), often divided into ’operational 
impacts’ and ’embodied impacts’. It must be assumed that, concurrently while the operational impacts are 
decreased, the importance of considering the embodied impacts gains more importance. As 28% of the total 
global energy-related CO2-emissions come from building operations, it can be considered the greatest 
contributor at the time within the Built Environment. 
 
Due to the impact from various stages of a building’s lifetime, over the years it has become more and more 
popular to apply Life Cycle Assessments (LCAs) on the built environment to assess the environmental 
sustainability. An LCA assesses, optimally, all stages regarding a building’s life cycle, covering raw material 
extraction, material production, construction, operation, demolition and end of life treatments. 
 
LCAs are performed either on building materials or on a building itself. Most often buildings are rated by 
using one of the established systems, e.g. DGNB, LEED, Green Star, or BREEAM. However, by using these 
rating systems, most of the current LCAs on buildings rely on relative comparisons, i.e. they are assessing 
whether option A or B is the best, but they do not take the actual limits, given by the earth we live on, into 
account.  
 
A recent Danish project investigated the use of the Environmental Carrying Capacity of the earth as a measure 
for the sustainability of buildings (Brejnrod et al. 2017; Brejnrod 2015). By assessing two case studies, a 
prevalent Danish building and a ‘best-practice’ building, they found that none of the buildings were 
sustainable, as they both exceeded several of the impact categories’ limits within the Environmental Carrying 
Capacity defined by (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b).  
 
Furthermore, when assessing the sustainability of a building, it is most often compared to similar buildings 
and/or to buildings within the same country. The responsibility of our impact is, however, a global 
responsibility. So how can our buildings be called ‘sustainable’ when their assessment does not relate to the 
actual environmental means of our earth? And how can we make countries ‘compete’ in optimising their 
impact shares from the built environment, when we compare to different measures? 
 
1.1. Project hypothesis 
Considering the above statements and observations, this MSc. thesis seeks to answer the following 
interconnected research questions: 
1. Assuming that Danish buildings are not sustainable, as suggested by (Brejnrod et al. 2017; Brejnrod 
2015), why might this be? Could this be caused by the fact that Danish buildings use significant 
quantities of ‘environmentally burdensome’ materials and/or that the buildings are adjusted and 
designed for Nordic utilisation and hence a challenging climate? What are the contributions of the 
operational and embodied phases of the building’s lifecycle? 
2. How does the absolute sustainability of Danish buildings compare with Australian buildings? This 
question is asked in context to question 1, determining the extent to which absolute sustainability of 
buildings might differ based on geographical, regulatory, technological and climatic changes.   
 
==PAGE:13==
 
Introduction 
 
Page 3 of 85 
3. Can modular-based construction techniques increase the sustainability-performance in an absolute 
perspective? This assessment will be based on a Danish modular-building design, applying various 
assessment scenarios: 
a. Country specific: 
i. Adjusting the Danish Modular design to Australian performance requirements, by 
adjusting insulation thicknesses and window types. 
b. Material dependent scenarios focusing on: 
i. Insulation (Cellulose, mineral wool or PUR/PIR) 
ii. Foundation (Concrete or steel screw pillar foundation) 
c. Production location: 
i. Production of modules locally (either in DK or AU) 
ii. Outsourcing production to  
1. China 
2. Eastern Europe (e.g. Ukraine/Lithuania) 
 
The building solutions, modular and conventional, will be compared through LCA analysis and a normalisation 
regarding to ‘Absolute sustainability’ (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b). Absolute sustainability assessment is based 
on the environmental carrying capacity, which considers the maximum pressure that we can apply the 
natural system, without risking irreversible changes.  
 
As mentioned in (Brejnrod 2015), the absolute framework of sustainable building is defined as: 
 
“… an assessment of the buildings impact in relation to the environmental carrying 
capacity, in contrast to today’s sustainability definition where an assessment of 
building sustainability is related to impacts of the current practice.” 
 (Brejnrod 2015) 
 
The hypotheses of the project are found through various steps. By assessing and comparing scenarios across 
Australia and Denmark, the respective impact patterns from buildings are expected to be different. Where 
the Australian impacts are assumed to be more significantly dominated by the operational phase, the Danish 
scenario is assumed closer to a 50/50 distribution between operational and embodied impacts, due to 
extensive focus on improving and thereby lowering operational energy needs. 
Considering the comparison between the prevalent building designs and the suggested modular building 
design, it is assumed that the modular building will be ‘more sustainable’ than the prevalent and hence closer 
to be deemed sustainable within the definition of absolute sustainability.  
 
By performing this research, novelty contributions are made through extending the research application of 
the absolute sustainability term and measures in the built environment, leading the consideration of a 
sustainable building in a quantifiable direction, including the resource limits of the earth. Further, few actual 
comparisons of prevalent/current building designs have previously been performed, most LCAs are done on 
exemplary buildings, which are however not widely covering the existing building method today.  
Additionally, the comparison between countries may help highlight different issues in need of focus, to 
improve the sustainability of the built environment. 
 
==PAGE:14==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts

2. Literature review 
2.1. Literature review methodology 
When assessing the literature for the present project, four main concepts have been considered: 
1. Environmental performance and rating of buildings in Australia and Denmark 
2. Life Cycle Analysis in Buildings  
3. Absolute sustainability and carrying capacity 
4. Modular buildings and environmental performance  
 
2.2. Sustainability and the Built environment 
According to (Eurostat 2015), the total GHG-emission per capita in Denmark was 9.01 tonnes CO2-eq which 
is slightly above the emissions from EU28 that are found as 8.74 tonnes CO2-eq average per capita. However, 
the number has decreased with ~34 % since 2000, where the emissions were 13.72 tonnes CO2-eq, which 
may be explained by improved building efficiency and the extensive focus on decreasing environmental 
impacts. The greenhouse gas emissions of Denmark and EU28 are shown in Figure 2. 
 
 
FIGURE 2 – GREENHOUSE GAS EMISSIONS OF DENMARK AND EU28 IN RESPECTIVELY 2000 AND 2015 ACCORDING TO  
(EUROSTAT 2015) 
 
Looking at (The World Bank 2013), the numbers look slightly different. The same patterns with a decrease 
per capita is found for Denmark, however, the total GHG-emission per capita in Denmark in 2013 was found 
as 6.8 tonnes CO2-eq. This is almost the same as measured in 1960, where the measures began. Australia’s 
total emissions per capita was by (The World Bank 2013) found as 16.3 tonnes CO2-eq, with the pattern being 
mainly increasing since 1960. The emissions are shown in Figure 3. 
 
FIGURE 3 – TONNES OF CO2-EQ EMITTED PER CAPITA IN RESPECTIVELY DENMARK AND AUSTRALIA ACCORDING TO (THE WORLD BANK 
2013).

==PAGE:15==
 
Literature review 
 
Page 5 of 85 
In Denmark, the building industry is accountable for approximately 40% of the total energy consumption (CSR 
gov 2012). This is aligned with the average for the world found by (UN Environment and International Energy 
Agency 2017). Australia in that matter have been found to ~20%A of the total energy consumption (Ball et al. 
2016). Looking at the emission of GHG, Australia’s building industry emits 18.1% of the total carbon footprint 
of the country (Yu et al. 2017).  
 
Even though the building industry is found to contribute percentagewise less in Australia than in Denmark, it 
must be noted that the overall GHG-emissions of the countries vary, where Australia’s is larger than 
Denmark’s. Finding contribution shares for the built environment to be ~20-40% of the countries’ total 
emissions is however still considered significant, and as found in (UNEP 2009), the industry is also estimated 
to be the one with the biggest potential for reducing emissions significantly, while still being cost-efficient. 
 
To implement actions to reduce these emission numbers and consider sustainability in the built environment, 
countries have executed various initiatives. Some of the most common methods are assessment tools and 
regulations.  
 
 Sustainability enhancing requirements to the Built Environment 
2.2.1.1. Official building regulations 
Most developed countries all have well-defined building codes for new buildings, and many of them are 
currently or have recently developed similar codes to cover refurbishments and renovations. Most of the 
building codes are performance based, which mean they are evaluated based on the buildings ability to stay 
beneath the set maximum limits within e.g. heating/ cooling. Further they put out energy requirements to 
equipment in the building such as elevators, pumps, ventilation, water heaters and heating/cooling systems 
(UNEP 2009). It varies from country to country, whether the application of the building code’s requirements 
is mandatory or volunteer. However, experience shows that making them mandatory almost always is more 
successful (UNEP 2009).   
 
As this project investigates buildings and their environmental impact in Denmark and Australia, the 
regulations of these two countries have been assessed. Analysis and written comparison of the two countries 
requirements and legislation can be found in Appendix 1, whereas an outline is found in Table 1.

2.2.1.2. Energy use assessment methods 
The energy use is measured in various ways across the countries, and in Australia, it varies across states and 
territories as well. Analysis and written comparison of the two countries requirements and legislation can be 
found in Appendix 2, whereas an outline is found in Table 2.

2.2.1.3. Other rating systems in the Built Environment 
Apart from regulatory rating systems and legislation, many green building rating systems have appeared over 
the last couple of centuries. Rating systems are developed by various authorities. One of the big actors are 
World Green Building Council, which is a global network with national Green Building Councils (GBC’s) all 
over the world (World Green Building Council 2017a) The mission of the World GBC, and underlying GBCs, is 
to help reaching the goals of the Paris Agreement (United Nations 2015) by; limiting global rises in 
temperature to 2°C, make sure all building are/have zero net emissions, and reduce the CO2 emissions from 
the construction and building sector with 84 gigatons, all by 2050 (World Green Building Council 2017b). 
 
Each national GBC has decided upon a rating tool for sustainable buildings, which are not interfered with by 
the World  GBC, except it has to comply with their Quality Assurance Guide (Towell 2015). A list of green 
building rating tools administered by Green Building Councils is given in Appendix 3. 
 
 
 
                                                          
B NatHERS= the Nationwide House Energy Rating Scheme 
C BASIX= Building Sustainability Index 
D For a detached and semi-detached dwelling 
E NABERS= the National Australian Built Environment Rating System 
==PAGE:17==
 
Literature review 
 
Page 7 of 85 
(Say & Wood 2008) listed the rating systems they meant to be the four main ones as Green Star, BREEAM, 
CASBEE and LEED. The study was however performed when only 12 countries had national GBCs, whereas it 
is above 70 countries today (World Green Building Council 2017a), for which reason the listing might be a bit 
outdated. The paper however, assessed the four given rating tools and an additional – Green Globes, to 
compare differences and similarities. They found that all surveyed systems varied in their way of assessing 
sustainability and they were limited in accounting for climate and cultural differences. A solution as ‘one-fits-
all’ is therefore not perceived as a good solutions, and they conclude that rating systems may be a driver to 
make designers ‘think green’, however they are not significantly enough to ensure a sustainable world and 
better performances (Say & Wood 2008). 
 
As these are inevitable a part of acceptance and markings of sustainability in the built environment, some of 
the assessment schemes in Australia and Denmark, and their implementation and measures of 
environmental sustainability have been assessed below. Both countries have a national GBC, but they also 
have independent rating tool/assessment schemes. 
 
2.2.1.3.1. 
Denmark 
Denmark’s GBC (DK-GBC) has chosen to adopt the German rating tool DGNB (Deutsche Gesellschaft für 
Nachhaltiges Bauen) for certification. However, some tools are developed to certify materials or performing 
LCAs on buildings, as a part of or alternative to complying with DGNB. Moreover, many developers tend to 
have their own system to  assess sustainability (Ullum et al. 2013). 
 
Some of the tools listed in Table 3 are more often used commercially or for large scale residential project, as 
the procedure can be rather comprehensive and expensive. Few of the tools are applied to smaller residential 
projects, however, sustainability is often introduced in these types of project by ‘just’ considering e.g. 
functional design, daylight considerations, minimise energy consumption (both in design and habits), indoor 
environment, adaptability of design, using environmental less burdensome materials (e.g. recycled 
materials), install renewable energy sources and reuse rain water, and/or assess the overall economy of the 
building life cycle (simplified, as this can be quite comprehensive as well) (Hansen 2017; Ullum et al. 2013). 
Short explanations/presentation of the tools can be seen in Appendix 4. 
 
Additionally to the tools listed in Table 3, some companies also use or comply with overseas tools such as 
LEED and BREEAM (COWI 2017; Rockwool 2017). 
 
DK-GBC is an independent brand, however connected to World GBC through using DGNB. They further keep 
themselves updated by working closely together and holding conferences every 2-3 years with other Nordic 
GBCsF (Olsen 2017).  
 
2.2.1.3.2. 
Australia 
The Australian GBC (GBCA) has chosen to use/develop a rating tool called Green Star. Additionally, to Green 
Star, other tools for specific materials, carbon emissions and/or LCAs are developed and used, which are 
listed in Table 3. Short explanations/presentation of the tools can be seen in Appendix 4. 
 
Additionally, to the tools listed in Table 3, it must be mentioned that NatHERS, NABERS and BASIX are 
assumed certification systems in Australia as well. Further, LEED is also used in some aspects (Getting to 
Sustainability 2012).

 Sub-conclusion – Differences of requirements 
In review of ratings, regulations and tools, summarized above and extended in Appendix 1-4, quite some 
differences between the countries appear. This may lead to different ways of assessing and improving 
performance, which might be discontinuous from the fact that we’re all aiming for the same goal and 
reductions, as global warming, impacts and emissions are a global responsibility (Miller 2007; Lynn 2015; 
Broto 2013).   
 
Considering the obligatory regulations, it is seen that some points align, however some vary greatly – 
amongst other requirements for thermal performance. Minimum requirementsG in Denmark for u-values of 
windows in Denmark are 1.1 W/m2K (Trafikstyrelsen 2015), whereas the Australian standard does not give 
an concise requirement, but list some worst case values. Worst case u-values for single glazed windows are 
given as 5.6 W/m2K and for double glazed windows 3.8 W/m2K (Australian Building Codes Board 2016).  
For e.g. walls, discrepancies occur, however not as large, as the u-values minimum requirement for Australia 
was found as 0.36 W/m2K and for Denmark as 0.3 W/m2K (Trafikstyrelsen 2015; Australian Building Codes 
Board 2016). 
 
Another discrepancy is the fact that the Danish legislation considers air-tightness of the buildings, which must 
be tested upon construction. A similar requirement was not found in the Australian legislation 
(Trafikstyrelsen 2015; Australian Building Codes Board 2016). 
 
Some of the assessment tools and methods, e.g. BASIX and BE15 align and much of the input information 
looks similar. However, the output is based on different measures, where the Danish version measures 
according to regulatory limits, BASIX in Australia measures against a benchmark. Both can though be 
considered dynamic in a way, as BE15 is changing along with regulatory tightening and BASIX’ benchmark 
must be considered decreased in benchmark updates along with buildings performing better and better, as 
it is based on the average building performance. 
 
CSR Australia, a Building Product company, made a range of research projects, and in an article, they stated 
some of the important matters to consider when wanting to design an optimal building. Things like heat 
(insulation in all building surfaces, also windows), airflow and tightness in the structure and moisture control 
were highlighted (Clarkson n.d.).  Some of these matters are also concerned in the volunteer certification 
available by (Passivhaus Australia n.d.). While none of this however is obligatory in Australia, it is very worth 
noticing that most topics are standard requirements in the Danish building regulation, as e.g. maximum 
allowable air flow volumes (Trafikstyrelsen 2015).  
 
The energy requirements for Australia have improved immensely, however the energy used for heating and 
cooling in Australia is still improvable (Roberts 2015). A factor that helps driving the development of saving 
energy, is the cost that has increased and is higher than other countries’ which has resulted in extensive 
research on energy improvements (Clarke n.d.). Further, project like the National Energy Efficient Building 
                                                          
G See tables in Appendix 1 
==PAGE:19==
 
Literature review 
 
Page 9 of 85 
Project (NEEBP), aiming to obtain better energy efficiency in renovations, additions and new buildings 
(Government of South Australia’s Department of State Development 2016), will strengthen the development. 
 
However, until these are implemented and regulated, the hypothesis of this project is that the Australian 
scenarios will be dominated by the operational phase due to a bigger energy use for heating/cooling, whereas 
it is assumed that the Danish/European scenario will be closer to having equal impact burden, due to 
optimized energy designs in buildings. This is, amongst other, based on the varying u-values, which e.g. 
results in Denmark using triple glazed windows, where 1 or sometimes double-glazed windows are mainly 
found in Australia. Differences in construction occur as well, with an exemplary illustration of thicknesses in 
Figure 4, based on minimum regulation requirements.

2.3. LCA in the Built Environment 
Life cycle assessments (LCAs) have been around for a couple of decades now, the first studies on 
environmental impacts being dated in the 1960s and 1970s. However, the first life cycle thinking appeared 
in the built environment in the 1980s (Buyle et al. 2013). 
 
An LCA is performed in order to identify, quantify and evaluate environmental impact of e.g. a service, activity 
or a product, over its entire life cycle: from extraction of raw materials till disposal. It can thus be used as a 
decision-making tool, enabling decisions-makers to base their opinions on quantified values.  LCAs are 
governed under ISO14040 and 14044 (ISO 14044 2006; ISO 14040 2006). 
 
Over the last couple of decades, assessments of the building industry’s impacts on the environment has 
gained more and more focus and LCAs of building are an extensively studied topic (Zabalza Bribián et al. 
2009), which can also be seen in the increased amount of publications regarding building LCAs (Geng et al. 
2017). Analysis’ are done in many ways, e.g. by assessing Embodied Carbon or Energy, performing an 
Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA), or looking at the entire life cycle with a Life Cycle Assessment (LCA).  
According to (Geng et al. 2017), most articles available from 2000-2014 were concerning energy, known as 
life cycle energy assessment, which was also suggested by (Ramesh et al. 2010). Others were, in the order as 
listed; materials, sustainability, carbon and technology (Geng et al. 2017). 
 
Most ’assessments’ of building sustainability put emphasis on operational impacts, with embodied impacts 
only rarely being considered, which is also found reflected in the fact that embodied impact is not part of any 
developed building regulations (i.e. building regulations only concern operational emissions – not embodied 
impacts). 
 
==PAGE:20==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 10 of 85 
The assessment method of this paper is chosen to be through an Environmental life cycle perspective. 
However, before performing the relevant LCAs for the project, previous LCAs performed within the Built 
Environment have been assessed through review papers. This, as the assessment of all building LCAs 
published would be an entire project itself, as more than 1900 papers have been published since 2000 alone 
(Geng et al. 2017). More than 24 review papers considering LCAs in the built environment have been 
published since 2009 (Anand & Amor 2017). In the reviewed articles, some papers were found to cover whole 
buildings, however, many of the papers written, concerning LCA in the built environment, focus on material 
comparisons. 
 
While the review papers were dated over various years, they seemed quite in agreement regarding the 
results of the reviewed studies. The review papers assessed LCAs within the built environment, however 
some of them also or only assessed Life Cycle Energy Assessment (LCEA) in the built environment. The review 
papers assessed are (Anand & Amor 2017; Buyle et al. 2013; Cabeza et al. 2014; Chau et al. 2015; Geng et al. 
2017; Ghattas et al. 2016; Khasreen et al. 2009; Petroche et al. 2016; Ramesh et al. 2010; Rashid & Yusoff 
2015; Sharma et al. 2011; Soust-Verdaguer et al. 2016; Zuo et al. 2017). Many of the reviews assessed the 
same literature, and hence the conclusions of the papers are relatively in concordance.   
 
(Cabeza et al. 2014) stated that most of the assessment papers they’ve reviewed seemed to be assessing 
‘exemplary’ buildings, which they describe as buildings, designed for optimum energy consumption (low-
energy buildings), and hence they point out a lack of studies on typical buildings, defined as buildings which 
are commonly being our habitat. They also found that studies are not equally performed throughout the 
world, and most studies performed are based in urban settings, whereas the rural settings are not well 
covered. Most case studies were produced in Europe or North America, with a few performed in Oceania and 
Asia, and few to none performed in South America and Africa (Cabeza et al. 2014). This is confirmed in the 
review performed by (Geng et al. 2017).  
 
Within the residential building industry (Petroche et al. 2016) found that 12 building Environmental LCAs 
were performed since 2000, 8 of them being from Europe and 1 from Australia. Hereof, 8/12 contained 
construction, use and end-of-life phases, 3/12 contained construction and use phase and 1/12 only covered 
construction. 
 
 Functional unit and lifetimes 
The functional units used in the various assessments differ from covering/person, ‘per housing unit or 
buildings’, /m2 referring to either net floor area, heated area, living area, gross floor area, floor area without 
detailed specification or usable floor area, or m3  as gross volume or heated volume (Sharma et al. 2011; Chau 
et al. 2015; Cabeza et al. 2014).  
 
(Ghattas et al. 2016) found that most of the papers assessed used a house as functional unit, using a base 
measure of the building’s footprint or floor area, which was agreed by (Anand & Amor 2017; Soust-Verdaguer 
et al. 2016). From this, it can be concluded that comparison between studies performed may be difficult, due 
to the diversity of functional units. 
 
(Rashid & Yusoff 2015) mentioned studies pinpointing the missing availability of reliable lifespan data, for 
which reason lifetimes are often based on literature or previous similar studies. All the review papers found 
various lifetime spans, however, Figure 5 shows that most of the residential LCA studies have a considered 
lifetime of 50 years.   
 
It is important to note that many of the review papers assess the same articles, for which reason the ‘input’ 
data is accounted for several times in several review papers. From the gathered data it can be concluded that 
==PAGE:21==
 
Literature review 
 
Page 11 of 85 
the main reference service lifetime, used for assessments within the built environment, is 50 years. The 
second most used service life times is however a bit fluctuating, due to the variability in considered cases in 
the review papers. 
 
(Ramesh et al. 2010) 
(Ghattas et al 2016) 
(Soust-Verdauger et al. 2016) 
FIGURE 5 – USED LIFETIME DISTRIBUTION. LEFT: (RAMESH ET AL. 2010), REVIEWING 46 CASE STUDIE OF RESIDENTIAL BUILDING FROM 
13 RESEARCH PAPERS. MIDDLE: (GHATTAS ET AL. 2016), REVIEWING 65 CASE STUDIES FROM THE STUDIES THEY REVIEWED. RIGHT: 
(SOUST-VERDAGUER ET AL. 2016), REVIWEING 24 CASES IN FROM 20 ARTICLES REVIEWED. 
 
(Cabeza et al. 2014) argues that most papers consider a lifetime of 50 years, they however highlight the 
importance of performing a parametric analysis with varying lifetime assumptions in order to evaluate if it 
will affect the results. And as (Anand & Amor 2017) states, lifetimes are most often assumed, as no 
appropriate calculations for this exist and data about actual life time of an assessed building will not be known 
until after demolition in x amount of years. However, some of their reviewed articles, based lifetimes on 
lifetime data for old buildings, but most studies base them on commonly used lifetime for similar types of 
cases and/or the expected lifetime of the main material in the building. Exactly due to these imprecise ways 
of defining the lifespan, (Anand & Amor 2017) also highlight the importance of a sensitivity analysis with 
differing lifespans. 
 
 System boundaries 
(Khasreen et al. 2009) stated that the life cycle stages mostly implemented in assessments were embodied 
energy of materials and building components, transport of materials and components from production to 
site, the use phase in terms of operational energy, replacement and maintenance, demolition, transport from 
site to waste treatment, and waste treatment. Assessments including material waste and water consumption 
were mentioned as occurring occasionally. They found that phases, or processes, like equipment 
transportation, construction of the building at site, and construction waste were generally not included. 
 
(Rashid & Yusoff 2015) mentions that End of Life (EoL) stages are included more often in recent research, due 
to the awareness of the potential benefits of recycling. They additionally mention that materials such as steel 
and aluminium are often accounted of as recycled, whereas concrete is downcycled into aggregate, and the 
rest of the materials are driven to a landfill. 
 
(Buyle et al. 2013)  note that life cycle stages most often excluded are waste factors, water use, 
transportation, and maintenance. Further, they state that due to a number of assumptions and exclusions, 
they would categorise some studies as being screenings, rather than detailed LCAs. 
(Chau et al. 2015) argues that some life cycle phases can be ignored due to their minor influence on the final 
impact results. In the reviewed papers, they found that demolition energy use accounts for 0.2% of the total 
primary energy consumption in various buildings. And even when assessing low-energy buildings, this 
number only rises to 0.3% (Chau et al. 2015).  
==PAGE:22==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 12 of 85 
A minimal importance of including transport is mentioned by (Buyle et al. 2013), who reviewed an article 
about triple glazed windows for a building being transported from Germany to Italy, and cork slabs being 
transported with both ship and truck over long distances, which had a minor impact contribution. From 
another one of the reviewed studies, they concluded that transport only has a significant contribution, when 
all the materials are transported over long distances, i.e. in cases where everything has to be imported from 
overseas (Buyle et al. 2013). 
 
(Ghattas et al. 2016) mention the variance of the reviewed studies within residential buildings, as they do 
not follow any one specific methodology or scope.  Variance occurred in almost every aspect of the studies, 
resulting in each study applying techniques and scope according to their unique considerations and 
conditions. Investigating some of the common factors studied, the paper concluded that most of the studies 
assessed either one or more buildings or building materials and that most buildings assessed were around 
100m2-200m2, in a set geographical area within one country. From this, they note the importance of impact 
caused by e.g. heating/cooling needs, and energy grid (Ghattas et al. 2016). A recent article by (Pomponi & 
Moncaster 2018) stated the variations of the use and application of LCAs could lead to limitations in 
comparing results and conclusions in different studies. 
 
When considering the scope of the studies, (Ghattas et al. 2016) noted that a significant range of applied 
lifetimes (they found numbers from 30-100 years) were impacting the results, both considering the energy 
use, but also the needs for renovation and maintenance. They further found a tendency indicating that the 
longer the applied lifetime, the more inclusions of significant renovation and maintenance were a part of the 
study. One of their other case studies argued the use of short lifetimes to be most relevant and usable to 
meet the current climate change mitigation goals, concluding that shorter assessed lifetimes will yield more 
useful results. (Ghattas et al. 2016) 
 
 Software used 
(Khasreen et al. 2009) presented the databases and tools available for respectively Whole Process 
Construction (WPC), and Building and Materials Component Combinations (BMCC), as shown in Table 4. But 
as they note; some of the data that they list may be almost complete or having people working hard towards 
completing them. However, due to the large amount of materials within the industry and construction 
techniques, and a constantly developing industry, none of the datasets or tools are able to assess a complete 
life cycle assessment of an entire building in detail. (Rashid & Yusoff 2015) come to the same conclusion, 
saying that no database can be seen as complete due to the great variety of materials, manufacturing 
processes, construction procedures, site locations, energy sources, supply assumptions etc.

From Table 4 and Table 5, and (Zuo et al. 2017) it can be concluded that none of them listed a comprehensive 
list, as they only  partially complement each other’s lists. For this reason, it is important to note that the tools 
listed in this section are not necessarily an exhaustive and complete list, but merely is given for an overview 
of possible and used tool for LCAs in the built environment. 
 
(Soust-Verdaguer et al. 2016) assessed how data was obtained in various papers, depending on the different 
life stages assessed in a lifecycle analysis, and the information has been collected in Figure 6. It must be noted 
that even though End-of-life and recycle stages along with water consumption are listed in the table, they 
were only addressed in very few of the reviewed studies. 
 
 LCI data in the Built Environment 
A review paper by (Martínez-Rocamora et al. 2016) assessed LCA databases that concerned construction 
industry. The study lists 12 databases; however, these are not exhaustive. For Europe, they list ecoinvent, 
ELCD database, GaBi database and the Plastics Europe Eco-profiles. Additionally, they list some databases for 
the US and some national databases for France, Spain, Sweden and Germany, these are not assumed relevant 
though. They mention that ecoinvent and GaBi are identified as the databases which appear most complete 
(Martínez-Rocamora et al. 2016). 
 
In summation, data are still being developed and no database is complete, as mentioned in e.g. (Rashid & 
Yusoff 2015; Khasreen et al. 2009). Especially the use phase in LCA modelling was found by (Soust-Verdaguer 
et al. 2016) to very often be estimated by the authors of the reviewed papers. 
(Islam et al. 2015) wrote a review paper on LCA and cost implications on residential buildings. In this they 
concluded that for application of LCI databases, at the current state, the most suitable applications of data 
would be ecoinvent in Europe, Athena for Canada and US, and AusLCI in Australia.  
 
A general outline of LCI data can be seen in Appendix 5.

 Impact categories considered 
(Khasreen et al. 2009) found that when LCA was applied to buildings, the most common impact categories 
assessed were global warming, ozone depletion, eutrophication and acidification. However, they also noted 
that the impact categories used in every study largely depended on the scope and goal of the study, along 
with data availability and impact significance.   
 
Even though many studies only concentrate around a couple of impact categories, (Chau et al. 2015) 
highlights the importance of considering other categories as well. One of the papers they reviewed found, 
that by using a renewable energy system, a building’s CO2 emissions could be reduced. However, other 
impact categories, like Human Toxicity Potentials and Freshwater Ecosystem Impacts, increased with 
respectively 25% and 100%.  
 
Another angle was found in the reviewed studies by (Soust-Verdaguer et al. 2016). They mention the 
importance of local priority, as some impact categories are regional rather than global. An example they 
found in a reviewed study was for a case study in Jordan, where it was estimated that the impact categories 
(Water Depletion, Climate Change, Acidification Potential, Human Toxicity Potential and Particulate Matter 
Formation) were the most important. However, if other regions or countries were to be assessed, these 
priorities may change.

 Case study results found in the literature 
(Cabeza et al. 2014) concluded that in most of the studies reviewed, it was concluded that the operational 
phase had the largest contributions, accounting for 80-85% of the total energy consumption. They therefore 
recommended future focus to be on optimizing the operational phase. However, they also reviewed studies 
where tendencies showed that low-energy building designs tended to have higher embodied energy 
compared to conventional building designs.  This is supported by (Buyle et al. 2013) who concluded that 
standard buildings showed a use phase contributing with 60-90% of the total impact. They also mention that 
due to regulations, there is a tendency of buildings becoming more energy efficient and thus the contribution 
division will see a change in the future of phase contributions. Examples of other phases that might end up 
contributing significantly are mentioned, such as the end-of-life phase, the choice of materials, and water 
use. 
 
In their review of life cycle energy analysis of buildings, (Ramesh et al. 2010) also concluded that the 
operational energy contributes the most, having a share of 80-90% of the total life cycle energy use. The 
embodied energy thus only accounted for 10-20%, and they found that demolition and other processes’ 
energy use was negligible. 
 
(Ghattas et al. 2016) notes as well that many of the existing studies are dominated by the use phase. They 
reviewed papers that found the more energy efficient the building, the bigger share the embodied energy 
will have. Some cases showed a contribution of 50% of the total energy use, though the most common results 
are still that the embodied phase contributes less than 10%.  
 
Uncertainties in the reviewed LCA studies have been touched on by several of the papers as well (Ghattas et 
al. 2016; Buyle et al. 2013; Ramesh et al. 2010; Soust-Verdaguer et al. 2016), mentioning the main issues to 
be: 
- 
Lack of accounting uncertainties as a severe problem 
- 
Availability of data or lack hereof, leading studies to use commercial data instead of primary data 
- 
Stochastic errors and variability of values describing outputs and inputs, which are caused by things 
like temporal variations, measurement uncertainties, and process-specific variations. 
- 
Applied lifetime assumptions due to dynamics of development. This may, amongst others, affect 
energy use as supply may change over time. 
- 
Applied energy and electricity sources, factors and values. 
- 
Exclusion of data, flows, phases etc. 
- 
Choice and potential mixing of databases 
 
Extended explanations on the uncertainties highlighted in the review-studies can be viewed in Appendix 6. 
 
 Sub-conclusion – LCA in the Built Environment 
After assessing review papers that have analysed the existing research papers on LCA and energy 
assessments in the built environment, it was found that most of the review papers come to similar 
conclusions.  
Most of the existing LCA studies of buildings use a service reference life of 50 years, and a functional unit of 
/m2 floor area. These values will be used in this project as well to ease comparison across case studies. 
However, to accommodate possible uncertainties or sensitivities, the building lifetime is tested with a 
lifetime of 120 years. 
 
As (Cabeza et al. 2014) concluded most studies to be on exemplary designs, a gap was found, which this 
project aims to fill. They mention a general lack of assessment of prevalent buildings in the design in which  
 
==PAGE:26==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 16 of 85 
we live today. This topic will be covered by part 1 of this report (by considering the reference cases), 
representing prevalent, standard building designs in respectively Denmark and Australia.  
 
The scopes of the existing assessments were found to vary considerably. This paper will aim at being as 
consistent and elaborate as possible, covering as many life cycle stages as possible, from cradle to grave. 
However, due to the natural time-limitations of this project, the allotment for data collection will be critical 
for this collection. As (Rashid & Yusoff 2015) mentioned, most studies do not assess EoL stages. This study 
will include EoL stages along with considering possible and relevant EoL treatments in the two countries, as 
practises may vary.  
 
Several of the review papers state that the mix of energy generation technology has a significant effect on 
the impact results (Petroche et al. 2016; Buyle et al. 2013; Ghattas et al. 2016; Ramesh et al. 2010; Chau et 
al. 2015). In a life cycle assessment study performed by (Rossi et al. 2012), where they compared case studies 
of residential buildings in different European cities, they found results to be mostly affected  by the electricity 
and energy mix of the country in question, which could reverse the conclusions if changed. 
The electricity grid inputs will be tested in this project as well, in order to consider the variation and sensitivity 
of this factor, as it is assumed that the results of the environmental LCAs of the buildings in respectively 
Australia and Denmark will vary due to different electricity supplies. Where Australia is mainly powered by 
coal, and Denmark is powered by mainly renewable energy (Ball et al. 2016; Energistyrelsen 2016), even 
within Australia the electricity grid changes, why the values for the state New South Wales have been used 
(Australian Government 2016). Figure 7 shows the electricity-production-grid contributions of respectively 
Australia, New South Wales and Denmark.  
 
 
FIGURE 7 – ELECTRICITY SUPPLY GRID FOR DENMARK, NEW SOUTH WALES AND AUSTRALIA (BALL ET AL. 2016; ENERGISTYRELSEN 2016; 
AUSTRALIAN GOVERNMENT 2016) 
 
As stated in several papers, the use of non-regional or local LCI datasets may impact the results in an 
erroneous way. Some of the reasons for these errors are mentioned to be the ‘natural’ variation of 
technology and production methods across borders along with energy sources (Lu et al. 2017; Martínez-
Rocamora et al. 2016). 
 
To minimise the erroneous results’ impact, the source of data in this project is ‘local’ databases to the furthest 
extent possible. For Denmark, the local database is assumed reflected in European data, using ecoinvent.  
 
For the Australian scenarios, the aim is to use Australian data to the furthest extent possible, through the 
database AusLCI. As the database is still limited in some construction areas e.g. steel materials, ecoinvent will 
be used to supplement those missing, as also done in (Islam et al. 2015). In order to check the sensibility of

2.4. Absolute sustainability 
In 2013 (Bjørn & Hauschild 2013) wrote an article concerning the difference between relative and absolute 
sustainability measures, and how these could learn from each other. They highlighted two of the main 
methods of assessing sustainability, aiming to decrease environmental impact in general, eco-efficiency and 
life cycle assessments, which together make up relative sustainability. As the absolute measure, they 
highlight the cradle-to-cradle concept, that aims at designing eco-efficient products, thereby not draining the 
resources of the earth (Bjørn & Hauschild 2013). They describe the difference between absolute and relative 
sustainability by: 
 
“Increases in eco-efficiency quantified by LCA can improve the relative sustainability of 
the product, but how much closer that has brought the product to the goal of absolute 
sustainability remains unknown”  
(Bjørn & Hauschild 2013) 
 
This quote also highlights some of the drawbacks of eco-efficiency, such as it being short-term oriented. 
 
As also mentioned briefly in previous sections, and as stated in (Brejnrod et al. 2017; Brejnrod 2015), most 
environmental assessment tools, for this project specifically within the built environment, measure the 
results against thresholds or benchmarks based on current practice. Even though this, over time, may 
improve our practises, as benchmarks will improve, it does not take into account the limitations given by the 
earth we live on, like concluded in (Bjørn & Hauschild 2013), about eco-efficiency and LCA. An absolute 
measure is therefore needed to assess whether we stay within the limits of the earth. 
 
World overshoot day is a term used to describe the day, when we, globally, have used more than the 
resources of earth that have been allocated for one year. It has become a re-occurring ‘event’, which falls 
earlier for each year, meaning that we are currently using more than we should, even considering what can 
be reused and recycled yearly. In 2017 the amount of earths needed to cover the consumption is estimated 
to be 1.7, which is an average estimate, as some countries use less, some much more (Suzuki 2017; WWF 
Denmark 2017). In 2017 the World-Wide Fund for Nature (WWF), released an article showing how many 
earths, we would need, if the entire world population lived like certain countries. Industrial countries are 
consuming way above the average earths needed, see Figure 8 for comparison (WWF Denmark 2017). 
 
The measuring of available resources is not a new phenomenon, and many researchers have been occupied 
with the topic. Measuring in terms of ecological or environmental footprints have been around for some time 
(Wackernagel & Rees 1996). It has often been used to promote products and is somewhat known to the 
public as an ‘easy’ way of understanding our impact on the environment.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:28==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts

DENMARK 2017) 
 
The ecological and environmental footprint is often calculated in various aspects, and can also be reflected 
as a personal footprint (WWF n.d.; Global Footprint Network 2017), depending on how much (and what) you 
consume in various situations of your everyday life. Some of the biggest contributors are found to be food, 
home and travel (WWF n.d.)., but these are only for personal consumptions. We also need to consider all the 
processes taking place for a common good, which we do not see directly in our everyday life, like 
construction, infrastructure etc. 
 
 Measurement of absolute sustainability in the Built Environment 
Studies examining absolute sustainability in the built environment appear limited. However, some are 
discussed below: 
 
In 2006 (Lowe 2006) performed a study addressing whether working limits could be defined in terms of  
global carrying capacity for the building industry. He claimed the reason for the study was a concern that 
environmental performance targets were probably more subjective than what they should be (Lowe 2006). 
In his paper, he lists five different approaches for absolute measures: Static equilibrium, Asymptotic 
approach, Integral of excess approach, Planned future approach and Developmental approach. Most often, 
and in his study as well, the approach used is the static equilibrium, where the resources of the earth are 
divided equally between the current human population of the world. He does however encourage the urgent 
consideration about the utopian nature of this. Furthermore, he mentions the issue about allocation of global 
==PAGE:29==
 
Literature review 
 
Page 19 of 85 
limits for human consumption and activity onto the construction industry, and suggests that we further the 
interconnection with other industries and technologies (Lowe 2006). 
 
(Lowe 2006) also concluded that he does not find it effectively possible to define absolute targets for the 
environmental strains of the building industry, as they do not come out unique, objective and in unambiguous 
metrics. He mentions that the closest to this, is concluding that several impact categories are currently 
exceeding the resources available and must hence be accounted unsustainable (Lowe 2006).  
 
(Bendewald & Zhai 2013) addressed a way of applying carrying capacity as a baseline when assessing the 
sustainability of buildings. They highlight the carrying capacity measure to be one possible absolute measure, 
when one wants to assess against a measure that is deemed sustainable based on a credible science 
(Bendewald & Zhai 2013). 
 
In their study, (Bendewald & Zhai 2013) defined three established carrying capacity measures of absolute 
sustainability, listing: 
- 
Ecological footprint: If a building consumes/emits more energy than what was previously emitted at 
the site, then a building is deemed unsustainable.  
- 
Net zero energy: Accounting for the energy consumed by the building, which should be 0 in order to 
be considered absolute sustainable. However, they pinpoint that this method has some drawbacks, 
these being not including embodied energy, and a lack of documentation of calculations. 
- 
IPCC reports: By basing the absolute measure on the reports from IPCC, emissions are controlled to 
not exceed the limit at which catastrophic effects of Climate Change will occur. 
- 
 
Their study developed a measure of net carbon flow from earth to atmosphere, where a building, in order to 
be ‘absolute’ sustainable, must be storing equally the amount of carbon or more than the original site did, 
prior to construction. The study only assessed absolute measures within carbon, but acknowledged the need 
for also covering other impact areas, e.g. within water, biodiversity, waste etc. They state that their study is 
not to be seen as a definitive measure, but more as a step towards a ‘more absolute assessment’ of building 
sustainability (Bendewald & Zhai 2013). 
 
Additionally, some uncertainties of the method are highlighted in the form of obtainable data and the fact 
that, in practice, a project can be made ‘more sustainable’ or ‘closer to the limit’, if the size of the site 
assessed is increased. This is seen as a weakness, because even though it might improve the performance 
relatively of the building, it will lead to urban sprawl which does not improve anything about the issue that 
carbon emission of the built environment are beyond the carrying capacity (Bendewald & Zhai 2013). 
 
 Environmental carrying capacity 
When expanding the scope for applications of carrying capacity measures for environmental assessments, a 
conference contribution by (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015a) presented the integration of planetary boundaries in 
life cycle assessment frameworks, which allows one to analyse absolute environmental sustainability of 
systems and products. 
 
They, as many others, conclude that LCA addresses sustainability in a relative matter, by comparing two 
products or systems and then being able to choose the one with the lowest impact, i.e. the highest eco-
efficiency. However, this still does not assess the performance according to the ecosystems’ limitations of 
resources (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015a).  
 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 20 of 85 
(Bjørn & Hauschild 2015a) introduce the definition of planetary boundaries as a suitable measure, as they 
are defined as the limits within which we must stay in order to assure the earth’s resilience and ability to 
regenerate. In their study, which later led to (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b), they present a way to link the 
planetary boundaries (PB) to impact categories within LCA, which again can be expressed as person 
equivalent measures for safe operating space (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015a). 
 
The term ‘planetary boundaries’ was first presented by (Rockström et al. 2009). They define the term as:  
 
“The space within [which] humanity can safely operate without interfering with the 
system of the earth and the biophysical processes and sub systems of the planet”  
(Rockström et al. 2009) 
 
The boundaries of the safe operating space of the planet are defined for chosen subsystems (listed in Table 
6) in risk of tipping over to a new state, which would be irreversible or difficult to reverse (Rockström et al. 
2009). They found that 3 of the given systems had already exceeded the safe operating space: Biodiversity, 
climate change and interference with the nitrogen cycle, with others of the systems nearing the boundaries 
as well (Rockström et al. 2009). This is illustrated in Figure 9.

The definition of a person equivalent in (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b), which links the planetary boundary 
definition by (Rockström et al. 2009) is given as: 
 
“A person equivalent is, in this context, defined as the maximum amount of 
environmental impact that an average person can cause when society as a whole 
must stay within the safe operating space.”  
(Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) 
 
In their integration for the framework, (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) further introduce and define some 
additional impact categories, which make the planetary boundaries compatible with midpoint impact 
categories from common LCA impact assessment methodologies within environmental sustainability.  Most 
of the categories are based on the ILCD characterisation factors (CF), however with a few exceptions.

 Sub-conclusion – Absolute sustainability 
As this master thesis project seeks to assess building sustainability in an ‘absolute’ term, the methods and 
thresholds given above are the base of calculation and normalisation of this study.  
 
As the developed normalisation references are given per person in total, an allocation will need to take place 
later in order to facilitate relevant person equivalents for the building industry/dwelling only. This will be the

baseline and reference of the comparisons in both parts of this project (part 1 considering the prevalent 
building design, and part 2 considering the modular buildings). 
 
2.5. Modularity  
Global population is increasing dramatically, which incurs a need for building more houses. According to (UN 
DESA 2017), the populations is prospected to grow from ~7.4 billion people in 2015 to 9.8 billion in 2050. 
Tendencies show that urbanisation is increasing rapidly along with this population growth. In 2017 54% of 
the global population lived in cities, and it is forecast that this amount will increase even more by 2045, with 
it then being around 6 billion people of the world’s population living in cities (Demographia 2017).  
 
One suggested solution for solving the increasing population and space issue has been to look at densified 
modular buildings. In addition to often being more efficient than conventional building methods, they are 
also praised for being more sustainable, flexible and fast productions (Minter 2014; ATCO 2017; Modular 
Building Institute n.d.; Arnel n.d.).  
 
While modular buildings have previously been seen as a temporary solution, often used for construction 
trailers or alike, a trend is now emerging for modularity to be a sustainable design strategy for multiple 
typologies from commercial buildings like schools and retails stores, to residential buildings in various heights 
and designs (Arnel n.d.; ATCO 2017). 
 
 Modular/off-site construction 
Modular buildings are, to a great extent, produced off site, most often in a factory which enables controlled 
and dry fabrication. The buildings are either produced as several components and then assembled on-site, 
or they are produced as one module when almost finished, only needing to be installed at the site (Kieran & 
Timberlake 2004; Kamali & Hewage 2016). 
 
(Kamali & Hewage 2016) defined the variables for off-site construction across 4 levels, as shown in Figure 10. 
Elements produced off-site will then be connected to other parts on-site to a greater or lesser degree, 
depending on what parts are still needed. Many levels, especially the smaller ones of pre-fabrication, are a 
part of our building procedures, but they are rarely considered in everyday life (Nenadovic & Andersen 2015). 
 
 
FIGURE 10 – LEVELS OF OFF-SITE FABRICATION ACCORDING TO (KAMALI & HEWAGE 2016) 
 
 
 
•Defined as elements in a building, 
e.g. windows, that are transported 
to site as units.
Component
•Building component of larger scale, e.g. 
cladding panels or wall sections.
Non-volumetric pre-assembly
•Building component of larger scale, e.g. bathroom pods. 
Defined by enclosing a usable space.
Volumetric pre-
assembly
•Complete modules, ready for commissioning. A building can be 
formed by one or several modules. 80-90% ready, as some minor 
on-site modifications and installations still needs to take place.
Modular 
construction
==PAGE:33==
 
Literature review 
 
Page 23 of 85 
One of the great benefits of using modular design and production strategies has been said to be the lower 
costs and less time-consuming process. Modular buildings are designed in dry and controlled environments, 
which eases the production, compared to on-site productions, which are often influenced by the weather. 
When producing the buildings off-site, it is also possible to prepare the site simultaneously, often resulting 
in time savings in the end; about 25-50% according to (Kieran & Timberlake 2004; ATCO 2017; Modular 
Building Institute n.d.; Arnel n.d.; Nenadovic & Andersen 2015; Aye et al. 2012) 
 
(Kamali & Hewage 2016) performed a critical analysis on strengths and weaknesses of modular buildings. 
From this they defined a list, as seen in Appendix 7. The main factors they found to be influencing 
environmental sustainability were: 
- 
Reduction of waste 
- 
Minimal site disturbance 
- 
Designing for assembly, disassembly, relocation and refurbishment 
 
 LCA of modular buildings 
Scientific research within LCA on modular buildings is still limited. A comprehensive critical review performed 
by) (Kamali & Hewage 2016) found 8 studies assessing LCA on modular buildings. From the review they 
concluded that most studies showed the modular building design to perform better than the conventional 
counterpart. In their review, they state that many tasks of building a modular and a conventional home are 
identical, but some significant differences occur, which may be opportunities for optimising and hence 
reducing material and energy consumption. (Kamali & Hewage 2016) 
 
Most of the studies found by (Kamali & Hewage 2016) assessed case studies, comparing modular and 
conventional building. The two commercial assessments they found analysed modular building alone. Three 
studies were on single family homes in respectively the US or UK (Kamali & Hewage 2016) and performed by 
(Quale et al. 2012; Kim 2008; Monahan & Powell 2011). Additionally, a study performed on a multi-family, 
eight story residential building performed by (Aye et al. 2012), was found relevant for this study, as it was 
performed in Australia.  
 
As outlined by (Kamali & Hewage 2016), most of the modular designs had wood as their main structural 
material, where the conventional buildings were build using either concrete or masonry. Some of the studies 
also made an ‘equivalent’ design, hence projecting designs from the modular to the conventional or vice 
versa after the best assumptions (Kim 2008). The  lifetimes assumed for the studies were either not stated, 
or they used 50 years (Kamali & Hewage 2016), reflecting the average lifespan used in LCA on buildings in 
general as found in section Functional unit and lifetimes. 
 
The study performed by (Kim 2008) found that the contribution patterns of environmental impacts looked 
relatively similar for both scenarios (modular and conventional), having a dominant operational phase above 
90%. Looking a bit closer at the impacts themselves however, he found that the energy consumption of the 
conventional building was 4.6% more than for the modular building. Likewise, the modular building emitted 
less greenhouse gases (3%). Considering waste generation, the conventional building was found to produce 
~2.5 times as much waste than the modular design (Kim 2008).  
 
Similar patterns were found in (Quale et al. 2012), where they found the GHG-emissions of the conventional 
building to be almost 6 tonnes CO2-eqI more than for the modular building. Additionally, they found that out 
of 10 assessed impact categories, the results were higher for the conventional building in eight of them, as 
illustrated in Figure 11. 
                                                          
I Emissions were given for the functional unit of the total house, with a floor area of 186m2 (Quale et al. 2012). 
==PAGE:34==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 24 of 85 
 
Another interesting finding was made when it came to the inventory stage. In their study, they found that 
the main structural material, wood, needed almost 25% more materials in terms of mass for the modular 
design, than for the conventional design, due to a need for structural stability in every singular model, and 
for structural partitions walls’ support for transportation. Additionally, they mention the waste accumulation, 
which often increases the need for ordering more primary material. By building modular homes in controlled 
environments and often producing more modules simultaneously, orders can be optimised, as opposed to 
the conventional building methods, where a lack of sufficient climate controlled and dry storage space results 
in inefficient material handling (Quale et al. 2012).  
 
(Monahan & Powell 2011) performed a study of embodied carbon and energy of a panellised modular home 
compared to 2 conventional houses. In their study, they found that the embodied carbon of the modular 
homes was 34% less than for a conventional home.  
 
 
FIGURE 11 – IMPACT RESULTS OF COMPARATIVE STUDY OF MODULAR AND ON-SITE BUILT BUILDINGS FOUND IN (QUALE ET AL. 2012). 
IMPACT RESULTS ARE SHOWN WITHIN A LOGARITMIC SCALE, AND IMPACT CANNOT BE COMPARED ACROSS CATEGORIES SHOWN. 
 
It is worth noting, when considering the given studies of residential modular buildings, that only the study by 
(Aye et al. 2012) performed a full life cycle analysis, whereas the studies by (Quale et al. 2012; Kim 2008) only 
considered the processes of material acquisition, module fabrication, site assembly and occupancy, and 
where (Monahan & Powell 2011) additionally excluded the operational phase, as it was a study of embodied 
energy and carbon. 
 
 Design for disassembly 
As mentioned in (Aye et al. 2012), modular buildings, or prefabricated constructions, have an advantage of 
often being easy to disassemble due to their structural nature, often having been assembled by steel or 
timber systems. This enables an easy disassembly, where the materials can either be reused, or they can be 
sorted correctly and sent to a suitable EoL treatment facility. This stands in contrast to conventional buildings, 
which are often constructed with concrete, where, even though the materials is often ground to aggregate 
used in either road fillings or new concrete production instead of gravel, it does not accommodate direct 
reuse (Aye et al. 2012).
