 Sub-conclusion – Modular buildings 
Even though few papers were found on modular building’s sustainability and comparative studies (Kamali & 
Hewage 2016), a literature gap is defined, as literature is still not as extensive as within subjects like LCA of 
conventional buildings. 
 
The papers found, performing similar assessments, performed relative assessments, finding that, relative to 
the conventional building method, modular buildings perform better. However, this project tries to clarify 
whether the modular building solutions is sustainable, in an absolute matter, considering the carrying 
capacity of the earth. 
 
2.6. Conclusions of Literature review and Hypothesis 
The tendencies of housing today are, overall, increasing housing units/floorage, which can either be 
detached, terraced houses or apartments. However, as found in (Brejnrod 2015), not even the ‘best practice’ 
house designs, as known in Denmark, are within acceptable boundaries of environmental impact in absolute 
terms. One of the suggestions in (Brejnrod 2015) are to decrease the living area per person in housing, which 
could decrease the overall environmental impact. 
 
This thesis thus seeks to investigate two aspects of sustainability of residential houses. The first part consists 
of a comparison between typical, prevalent, in-situ built, detached, one-family houses in respectively 
Denmark and Australia. This comparison aims to identify differences between impact patterns and to suggest 
possible directions for improvements in the represented countries of case studies. 
 
Second part of this project aims to investigate one tendency of development towards building more 
sustainably. It is investigated whether a modular building design could be a solution, considering both the 
modularity and densification of living. The comparison is based on a Danish design but will be customised to 
fit simplified Australian requirements.  
Lastly, the results of the two parts of this study will be compared through a discussion. The literature review 
showed that similar assessments have not been done before; however, a few subcategories of this project 
have been explored in other literature.  
 
It is suspected that the operational impact, for both the prevalent and modular case studies will be higher 
for the Australian cases than for the Danish ones. Furthermore, it is expected that the modular designs will 
appear to be closer to being deemed ‘absolute sustainable’ than the conventional cases. 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:36==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 26 of 85 
3. Methodology 
3.1. LCA Methodology 
 Goal definition 
This study has two main goals. The first is to determine to what extent national factors, like building traditions 
and regulations, affect the environmental impacts from conventional buildings in Denmark and Australia. The 
second goal is to assess the impact modular construction has on improving the environmental performance, 
compared to conventional buildings.   
 
These assessments are to be measured in terms of ‘Absolute Sustainability’, and once environmental impact 
results have been found, a relative comparison of the two building methods, conventional and modular, is 
performed, to see whether the specific case-studies of modular housing may be a solution for more 
sustainable development.  
 
 Functional Unit 
The functional unit of the project is set as the habitable floor area of a single-family home over a given lifetime 
of 50 years. The final impact results are given in the unit impact/m2/year. 
 
 System Boundaries 
As this study contains both a relative scenario comparison and an assessment of absolute sustainability of 
the buildings, it aims to include as many life cycle stages as possible. Often, when performing relative 
(benchmarking) sustainability comparisons, life cycle stages are neglected due to assumptions of similarity, 
as e.g. in (SBi 2015).  
 
When assessing the absolute sustainability, i.e. the total impacts of the scenarios, the scope of (Brejnrod 
2015) is followed, adding additional transport stages, due to the change in the process scheme of modular 
buildings.  Furniture and appliances in the buildings are not accounted for in this study. The system boundary 
of this assessment is shown in Table 8, based on (BS EN 15978 2011). The included stages are marked with 
green. Additionally, to the categories listed by BS EN 15978:2011, material waste, land conversion, land use, 
and use stage transportJ has also been included.

As Australia is divided into several unique climate zones, the assessed regional boundary is an eastern suburb 
in Sydney, in the state New South Wales (NSW). The climate zone here is defined as climate zone 5, ‘warm 
temperature’, according to the NCC. 
 
To align and simplify scope comparison, the SfB system (HFB 2011) has been used to define material 
categories in the bill of quantities. The categories included in this assessment are as listed in Figure 12.

 LCI datasets/databases 
The data used in this project is found in two different databases, both applicable with OpenLCA (Green Delta 
2006). A short explanation of the databases used can be seen in Appendix 8. The databases have been chosen 
based on findings in the literature review, and in order to present the most accurate and relevant data 
possible for the geographic differences in the project.  
 
However, in order to align datasets used in the Danish scenarios, it has been deemed more accurate to use 
consistent data, and therefore average European and swiss data from ecoinvent 3.3 (ecoinvent 2016) have 
been applied. For the Australian cases, AusLCI (ALCAS 2011a) has been applied to furthest extent possible. 
Due to limited relevant datasets available, these have been supplemented by the use of ‘Rest of World’ (RoW) 
datasets from ecoinvent. 
 
 Impact categories 
The impact categories assessed in the project are given by (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b), as they have paired 
normalisation factors for absolute sustainability for the impact categories below. The carrying capacities are 
based on scientifically sound thresholds from literature, however, some of them are expressed in metrics, in 
accordance with recommendations by ILCD, others are based on alternative linear characterisation factor 
(CF) modelling. The impact categories not based on ILCD are: Terrestrial acidification, Terrestrial 
eutrophication, Land use in both soil erosion and biodiversity, and water depletion (Bjørn & Hauschild 
2015b). 
 
==PAGE:38==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 28 of 85 
As the normalisation references are based on various CF models, three possible ways of assessing and 
applying the references are found through personal communication with Anders BjørnK, as the normalisation 
references are not directly applicable to ILCD midpoint results (CF compatible), even though units may appear 
similar. Three ways of accommodating this was found: 
 
1. Importing the relevant CFs into the LCA modelling tool, enabling the possibility to calculate 
characterised impact  values, on which the normalisation reference given by (Bjørn & Hauschild 
2015b) can be applied. 
2. Modified normalisation references, estimated into compatibility with ILCD for all 11 references are 
developed by (Sala et al. 2016). These are however ‘only’ developed based on the global 
normalisation references in (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b). 
3. Reducing the number of normalisation references applied to the ones compatible directly with ILCD.  
 
For this project it was chosen to apply option 3, due to the main impact categories applied on environmental 
impact building assessments being covered and ILCD compatible in the normalisation references, see Table 
9. The LCIA methodology in this project is chosen as ILCD 2011 midpoints, which is the available method in 
openLCA. 
To check sensitivity of this application choice, the global ILCD modified normalisation references found by 
(Sala et al. 2016) are applied to case studies 1-4, with caution due to awareness of lack of regionalisation of 
these modified references. 
 
TABLE 9 – APPLIED NORMALISATION REFERENCES GIVEN BY (BJØRN & HAUSCHILD 2015B) AND RESULTS OUTPUT UNITS IN ILCD 2011

Ozone Formation, as differences occur in the units, as seen in Table 9.

dissimilar units, however the conversion factor was found as 1.

Short introductions of the impacts categories along with environmental effects can be seen in Appendix 9. 
 
 Normalisation 
The normalisation of the impacts is found according to absolute sustainability, with consideration of the 
carrying capacity of the earth. Each impact category has an allocated carrying capacity for the buildings type, 
as calculated in section 3.6. 
 
To find the normalised impacts within each category, equation (2) is used. 
 
 
 
𝐼𝑁,𝐵,𝑖=
𝐼𝐵,𝑖
𝐶𝐶𝐵𝑇,𝑖
 
(2) 
Where 
𝐼𝑁,𝐵,𝑖 = Normalised impact of the building 
𝐼𝐵,𝑖 = Impact of the building 
𝐶𝐶𝐵𝑇,𝑖 = Carrying Capacity allocated for the building type (Allocation factor is defined in section 3.6) 
 
3.2. Case studies 
The project investigates four case studies across several scenarios. As found by (Brejnrod et al. 2017; Brejnrod 
2015), the prevalent building methods for one-family, detached dwellings in Denmark do not comply with 
the earth’s carrying capacity.  
 
In part 1, the same case house for Danish prevalent buildings as used in (Brejnrod 2015; Brejnrod et al. 2017; 
SBi 2015) is assessed in this study, in order to put up a reference case and enable a comparison of studies 
with pre-published literature. Furthermore, a prevalent Australian building, with the design provided by 
(National Standard Pty. Ltd. 2014), is assessed. This design is considered as national ‘standard’ in the 
Australian context. This comparison answers the first research question, trying to determine to what extent 
climate and the associated specifics of local building requirements of e.g. insulation, would have an impact 
on absolute sustainability. 
 
Part 2 investigates whether modular buildings might be an answer to lowering the environmental impacts, 
by complying with the carrying capacity limit. The design of the modular building is based on a design 
provided by the Danish company HusKoncept.  
 
 Part 1 – Prevalent building methods 
3.2.1.1. Danish standard building 
The Danish reference building is based on the building design given by the Danish Building Research Institute 
(SBi) (SBi 2015) , and also assessed by  (Brejnrod et al. 2017; Brejnrod 2015). It is a one-story, detached, 
single-family house with a net-area of 128m2 P. Energy simulation modelling of the building are based on 
drawings of the case study, remodelled by (Sohn et al. 2016). 
The house contains of a kitchen, living room, four bedrooms, dining room, a utility room/scullery and two 
bathrooms. 
 
                                                          
P As assumed in (Brejnrod 2015), based in SBi background report’s (SBi 2015) given gross area of 149m2 and the ground 
deck area given by (SBi 2015). 
==PAGE:40==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 30 of 85 
 
FIGURE 13 – ILLUSTRATIONS OF LAYOUT AND FACADE OF THE DANISH STANDARD BUILDING ASSESSED BASED ON (SBI 2015)   
 
A bill of quantities of the Danish prevalent building is found in Appendix 10, and is mainly based on (SBi 2015). 
Some additional information about installations have been based on the ‘Upcycle House’ in (SBi 2015) by 
(Brejnrod 2015). The bill of quantities is structured according to the SfB-system, developed for classification 
in the Swedish standards within the construction industry, but spread throughout other countries and 
approved by Commission Internationale du Batement (CIM) (HFB 2011). 
 
3.2.1.2. Australian standard building 
The Australian conventional building is based on the benchmark building found in the National Standard of 
Australia (National Standard Pty. Ltd. 2014) . It is a one-story, detached, single-family house with a net-area 
of 198m2 Q. The house contains a kitchen, living room, four bedrooms, office and two bathrooms. 
 
 
 
FIGURE 14 – ILLUSTRATIONS OF LAYOUT AND FACADE OF THE AUSTRALIAN STANDARD BUILDING ASSESSED  (NATIONAL STANDARD 
PTY. LTD. 2014)  
 
A bill of quantities of the Australian prevalent building is found in Appendix 11, and is based on (National 
Standard Pty. Ltd. 2014). The bill of quantities is adapted to the SfB-system for consistency and for 
comparisons. The bill of quantities includes the garage/parking area, as this is a material burden to the house. 
However, the area assessed as per the functional unit is habitable floor area, so this area is excluded from 
this definition. 
 
 Part 2 – Modular building 
The modular building is based on a design provided by HusKoncept. It is a two-storey, terraced design, 
containing 6 single-family units, each with a net-area of 99m2. 
Each unit contains kitchen, living/dining room, stair landing, 2 bathrooms and 3 bedrooms.  
 
A bill of quantities structured according to SfB-system of the modular building is found in Appendix 12 and is 
based on drawings and price calculations/masses given by HusKoncept. The flagship design for the company 
is based on terraced houses.  
                                                          
Q Found as ‘the total habitable floor area, internal dimensions’ in (National Standard Pty. Ltd. 2014).

3.2.2.1. Country dependency/energy requirements 
To modify the modular Danish design for the Australian context, the HusKoncept house has been adapted. 
For simplicity, only the building fabric properties are adjusted (insulation in the envelope and window 
specification). Other aspects like e.g. load requirement differences, design requirements (e.g. cyclonic areas 
etc.), construction methods etc., are not considered in this project. This means, maintaining the floorage and 
building design intact, to simplify the adjustments. A bill of quantities is found in Appendix 13. 
 
To adjust the windows, the design of the vertical windows is changed from triple glazed windows to double 
glazed windows. The roof window is maintained identical as a double-glazed window in both countries. 
 
Additionally, the thicknesses of wall, deck and roof insulations have been altered from requirements given in 
the Danish design, which are complying with Danish legislation. The needed insulation thicknesses for the 
Australian scenario was calculated based on the minimum u-values listed in the Australian building regulation 
(Australian Building Codes Board 2016), the relevant values are shown in Table 10. 
 
The U-values of the modified construction part in the Danish modular design are calculated according to 
DS418 (Dansk Standard 2011) and found as shown in Table 10. The calculation can be seen in Appendix 14.

3.2.2.2. Material dependency 
To assess how various material exchanges, compare and influence the environmental impact of the building 
design, some sub-scenarios have been set up for assessment. 
 
3.2.2.2.1. 
Insulation 
For the design, cellulosic insulation has been used. However, to assess whether this has a significant influence 
on environmental impacts, assessments using PUR/PIR and mineral glass wool are also undertaken. The 
modification is done by altering the thickness needed of the insulation material, to comply with the obtained 
u-value in the base-cases for modular buildings of either Denmark or Australia. The operational energy 
consumptions are therefore assumed fixed. The thermal conductivity on which the modifications are based, 
are shown Table 12. Excluding the effects on other material quantities and floorage in this project caused by 
insulation material thicknesses is thus considered a limitation of this approach.

As the heat conductivity of glass wool is the same as the cellulosic insulation, the thickness is kept intact. 
However, the total mass input is changed due to a different density. As PUR/PIR was found with a different 
heat conductivity, the thickness of insulation was changed as well, along with the density. The found 
roof/ceiling thickness has also been applied to the internal floor/vertical module partitioning (deck, according 
to the SfB-system), as the main thickness of insulation are similar in these constructions. Values are rounded 
up to the nearest 10. 
 
3.2.2.2.2. 
Foundation 
The original design is suggested using a pillar foundation of concrete. As an alternative, a design using steel 
screw pile foundation is investigated as this is suggested as a less environmental disturbing method along 
with being quick and easy. Masses applied in the scenarios, respectively concrete or steel screw pillars are 
listed in Table 13. The masses are given for a block of 6 units in the tables but scaled in the inventory for the 
functional unit.

3.2.2.3. Production dependency 
Outsourcing has indisputable been a process taking place in many industries, often due to a decreased cost 
of labour in developing countries compared to developed western countries. A scenario of outsourcing is 
assessed compared to regular productions in the respective country of analyses (Denmark or Australia). Due 
to lack of available adequate data, these scenarios are assessed solely by assessing the transport distances 
increased by outsourcing/overseas production, along with the change in electricity and energy grid. 
 
The production locations are as listed below: 
1. Production of modules locally (either in DK or AU) 
2. Outsourcing production to  
- 
China – for the Australian modular case study 
- 
Eastern Europe (e.g. Poland/Lithuania) – for the Danish modular case study 
 
The applied distances are shown in section 3.4.3.

are shown in the bills of quantities in Appendix 10-13 and as a comprised table in Appendix 28.

comprehensive data, which induced a need for assumptions and simplifications. The approach chosen, was

Energy consumption 
As the window composition has been divided into materials, a process considering the production of the

consumption during window assembly has been neglected in this project. LCI dataset as representative for 
window components have been aimed used.

3.4.1.4. Finishing treatments and completion 
3.4.1.4.1. 
Painting 
 
It is assumed that all inner surfaces (walls and ceilings) of prevalent buildings, even though not listed in 
references, are painted with at thickness of 0.06mm paint (BYG-ERFA 1996). Filler is assumed same thickness 
for simplification. 
For the modular design, a quantity in kg was provided in the calculations, which is applied to the BoQ. 
 
==PAGE:47==
 
Methodology 
 
Page 37 of 85 
3.4.1.4.2. 
Flooring 
Floor areas in the modular buildings are assumed from calculation sheets given by HusKoncept, given for 
respectively areas of tiles and wooden flooring. 
In the two reference cases, volumes/weights of the flooring are given as a set value for respectively wooden 
flooring in the Australian reference case, and wooden flooring, tiles and carpet for the Danish reference case. 
 
3.4.1.4.3. 
Stairs 
The stairs in the modular building design are estimated based on correspondence with a representative from 
a Danish staircase manufacturer, Schack Trapper (Rasmussen 2017). Estimations along with mass calculations

of a module. With the dimensions of one module 12m*5m and height of respectively 3.3 and 3.7m, the total

3.4.2.3. Material lifetimes 
As stated in section 3.1.2, the functional unit of the study is 50 years. However, an additional assessment is 
made for 120 years, to see how the assessed lifetime influences the results. 
 
The single material’s lifetimes in the buildings are assessed according to (SBi 2013) . However, for some parts 
of this are neglected. This occurs for materials which are placed in between other materials with longer 
lifetime. It is then assumed that the middle materials, despite of the shorter lifetime, is replaced when the 
first outer material is replaced. An additional exception to this is the paper wool insulation, which is assumed 
sprayed into the construction, therefore not requiring comprehensive dismantling of structural parts to be 
performed. 
 
Furthermore, supporting structures in the buildings are assumed to stay throughout the entire lifetime of the 
building instead of being replaced within the materials specific given lifetimes.  
 
Some materials were not immediately located in the literature from (SBi 2013), for which reason lifetimes 
were assumed from other resources. These materials, along with reference and assumed lifetime are shown 
in Table 22. 
 
TABLE 22 – ASSUMED LIFETIME FOR MATERIALS NOT COVERED IMMIDEATELY IN (SBI 2013) 
MATERIAL 
ASSUMED LIFETIME 
REFERENCE 
Roofing felt 
20 years 
(Miljøstyrelsen 2001) 
Paper wool 
50 years 
(Fynholm et al. 2014) 
 
When assessing the lifetimes of the windows, varying lifetime of each component has been addressed. 
However, it is assumed that the entire window is replaced upon ‘expiration’ of the glass/pane. Hence after 
25 years all window materials are assumed exchanged/replaced. 
 
The lifetimes applied are assumed similar across construction methods and countries. This should however 
be noted as an uncertainty, which, if more time was available, should be accommodated by researching 
country and construction specific material lifetimes.

3.4.2.3.1. 
Materials for replacement and maintenance 
Only materials exposed to the ambient environmentZ are assessed for replacement and maintenance. The 
materials which have been ascribed maintenance and have increased material consumption are seen in 
Appendix 18. The maintenance/replacement materials are modelled individually in the LCA model, as these 
will vary according to assessed life times of the building. 
 
3.4.2.4. End of Life treatments 
End of life treatments of the materials are based on current process data, not estimated processes occurring 
at the actual expected end of lifetime of the buildings assessed. This might incur an uncertainty, which is 
checked through a sensitivity analysis on input sensitivity. However, this method is chosen, due to greater 
uncertainties of future processes and development in the building industry. Many papers have investigated 
future construction and demolition waste management and how to handle it in the future by mainly dynamic 
modelling, they are however not implemented in this study. Examples of some of the studies, most of them 
concluding that a decrease in illegal waste dumping and increased recycling and reuse rates will in the future 
largely be driven by application of penalties and economic controlling, can be found in (Toft et al. 2017; Bohne 
et al. 2008; Muller 2006; Yuan & Shen 2011; Azhari et al. 2014; Di Maio et al. 2017). 
 
As the case studies are performed across two countries, various EoL treatments naturally occur. However, 
where exact data was not found, common assumptions for EoL treatment were applied. Furthermore, as 
waste treatment process datasets were limited, some assumptions needed to be applied, disabling the 
possibility to follow industry procedures. General discussion of waste treatment practices in both countries 
can be seen in Appendix 19. 
 
3.4.2.4.1. 
Modelled EoL treatments  
As mentioned earlier, not all actual processes happening within waste treatment in the built environment 
have representing LCI datasets. For this reason, simplification regarding the EoL treatment have been 
necessary. Appendix 20 summarises recycling rates and incineration percentages applied for treatments or 
materials, were they are respectively applicable, and discusses some common application assumptions for 
materials due to limitations of available data. For recycling and incineration accreditation of avoided products 
are further accounted for. The amount/material not considered recycled and/or incinerated, are assumed 
into landfill.  
The final EoL treatment allocated to materials in the case studies, can be seen in the inventory lists in 
Appendix 21 as allocated in the bill of quantities in Appendix 10-13.

3.4.4.1. Construction energy 
Due to scarce actual data of the construction energy use of the case studies, assumptions have been appli-
ed for both modular and conventional buildings. Using (Quale et al. 2012), values for energy use have been 
determined as shown in Table 26..  
 
The study of (Quale et al. 2012) provides/estimates energy use within electricity on/off site, gasoline for 
equipment use, and fuel, propane and natural gas consumption for heating purposes. The consumption is 
given in respectively kWh and MMBTUBB for the functional unit; a house of 186m2. Where consumptions 
ranges were given, the average consumption was used. By using this information, the energy use within each 
category is transformed into energy use per m2, which again is scalable onto the various case studies. The 
Australian scenarios/case studies are assumed with no need for heating, and hence only electricity and 
gasoline are consumed. 
 
However, as the building designs assessed in (Quale et al. 2012) are of wooden design, whereas the 
conventional design of this project are brick houses, the values are applied with caution and sensitivity of 
these data input are checked during modelling.

3.5. Model validation 
A validation of the model was undertaken according the previous study by (SBi 2015; Brejnrod 2015). The 
validation is done do ensure no major modelling mistakes have occurred, but also to obtain an idea of the 
deviations occurring, based on LCA tool and background data used, when assessing studies with somewhat 
similar prerequisites. 
 
The modelling of the Danish reference case has been based on the previous study by (Brejnrod 2015). 
However, some data have been altered for this project, to expand the scope to cover the a more 
comprehensive boundary. In order to accommodate this expansion, the inputs for the validation have been 
based on (Brejnrod 2015) for the Danish reference design.  
 
3.6. Allocation of Carrying Capacity  
How to assess how much of the earth’s resources should be allocated to the buildings is assessed below. It is 
however important to notice that these are ‘only’ suggestions on how to allocate the carrying capacity, and 
the perception of how to assess the ‘fair share’ of earth resources for the built environment may vary, 
depending on the viewer and researcher assessing it.  
 
The allocation method has been based on an economic allocation based on household consumption, 
however, other methods were discussed in the literature e.g.: 
- 
The allocation according to current environmental pressure, as performed in (Brejnrod 2015; 
Brejnrod et al. 2017). 
- 
Time allocation, considering the time spend at various services, e.g. home, work, groceries etc. 
- 
Waste allocation, considering the production of waste and waste flow contributions. 
 
None of these additional methods were applied due to either no comprehensive data for both case study 
countries, or due to limited scope, e.g. would an arguable significant process of food disappear when 
allocating according to time spend, and indirect processes would disappear when considering waste 
treatment. 
 
Basing the carrying capacity on the literature by (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) in section 2.4, the carrying 
capacity was allocated for person equivalent, based on the assumption of equal distribution of the resources 
of the earth. This means, every person in the world obtaining an equal share of environmental capacity at 
their disposal. This was done, by taking the entire environmental capacity and dividing this with the total 
amount of inhabitants of the earth.  
 
Considering the impacts caused by human beings, there are two types of impacts; direct and indirect. 
Examples of the direct impacts are when we’re driving our car around, the operation of our house or the 
consumption of food and goods. However, the indirect may often be neglected, which are caused by common 
or public processes not linked directly to every human being, examples being; construction of the roads we’re 
driving on or production of export goods which increase the national GDP. 
 
==PAGE:54==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 44 of 85 
Considering these direct and indirect impacts, an allocation is performed to define how much of the person 
equivalent carrying capacity can be ‘used’ for residential purposes. As illustrated in Figure 17, based on 
(Brejnrod 2015), there are several steps to this allocation:  
 
1. Earth’s resources need to be divided to the earth’s population. This is suggested how to be done by 
(Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b), which is used for this study.  
2. When the share available for one person is found, the share of respectively indirect and direct impact 
from a person must be found.  
3. Considering the share of direct impact from a person, a certain percentage of this is caused by 
housing.  
4. As many forms of housing exists, this is further needed to be divided, as this project considers one-
family dwellings, for which reason the share of this type of housing is needed found. 
 
This stepwise method, is the approach used for this study in order to allocate the share of carrying capacity 
for this type of case studies. 
 
 
FIGURE 17 – ALLOCATION OF CARRYING CAPACITY FOR A RESIDENTIAL HOUSE AS ILLUSTRATED IN (BREJNROD 2015) 
 
The next step is to consider the total amount of carrying capacity for a dwelling. This is done because mostly 
more than one person lives in a dwelling, and the residential type obtains the share of resources available 
for more than one person. Using the terminology from (Brejnrod 2015), the equation used to calculate the 
annual carrying capacity available for a dwelling for a specific impact category is given as: 
 
 
 
𝐶𝐶𝐵𝑇,𝑖= 𝐶𝐶𝑃𝐸,𝑖∗𝐴𝐻𝐻,𝑖∗𝐴𝐻𝑑𝑤𝑒,𝑖∗𝑅𝑎𝑣𝑒 
(3) 
Where 
𝐶𝐶𝑑𝑤𝑒,𝑖 = Carrying capacity allocated to the dwelling 
𝐶𝐶𝑃𝐸,𝑖 = Carrying capacity for a person as defined by (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) 
𝐴𝐻𝐻,𝑖 = Allocated share of person equivalent to household 
𝐴𝐻𝑑𝑤𝑒,𝑖 = Allocated share of household to a dwelling 
𝑅𝑎𝑣𝑒 = Average number of residents in a dwelling 
 
The allocation method chosen for this study is the economic allocation, which considers the current share 
of the specific activity/product of the total GDP. As this study considers both Danish (European) and 
Australian cases, the allocation method is performed twice – once for every country. The allocation 
calculation and division can be seen in Appendix 27. A summation of the key numbers for the allocation 
values is shown in Table 31.  
. 
 
 
==PAGE:55==

4. Results 
The following section contains quantitative results of both main case study modelling and sensitivity 
scenarios. The model input values for main case scenario 1-8d, is tabulated in Appendix 28.  
 
The results shown in the following sections are aggregated results; they thus contain all the included lifecycle 
stages of this study, along with EoL treatment benefits. This is mainly due to modelling limitations, causing 
difficulties in subtracting the impacts and contributions from each other to disaggregate the results. 
 
4.1. Carrying capacity for single family housing 
The carrying capacity for a dwelling is calculated based on the normalisation factors found in Table 7 in 
section 2.4.2 and the allocation found is section 3.6. Based on correspondence with Anders Bjørn, the 
normalisation reference applied for Australia is the global reference, and the one for Denmark is the 
European one. This is further backed by the argument that the global normalisation reference is based on a 
lower population density compared to Europe, which is reflected in the Australian population density which 
is smaller than in Denmark, as shown in Table 33. This application assumptions are hence checked for 
sensitivity by applying the European normalisation reference on Australia and global reference on Denmark. 
Further, it is important to note that the population density is based on the entirety of Australia, whereas the 
population in reality mainly lives nearby the coast and hence most of the inner country is rural and non-
inhabited. This may lead to skewed population allocation. 
 
TABLE 33 – POPULATION DENSITY OF COUNTRIES

The results shown in the following section are based on ecoinvent datasets only, due to some modelling 
uncertainties and issues with AusLCI, which are discussed in section 4.4.1. 
 
 Absolute sustainability assessment 
As this project aims to do an absolute assessment, the resulting impacts are normalised in regard to the 
allocated values found in section 4.1, considering how much of the carrying capacity is available per m2/yr. It 
was found that none of the assessed scenarios could be deemed absolute sustainable, as they all exceeded 
the allocated impacts limits given as normalisation references. Case 1-4 are shown in Figure 18, where 100% 
represents the threshold. The scale on the figure is logarithmic in order to assess all scenarios’ exceedance. 
 
The normalised values of each of the case scenarios are tabulated in Table 35. The colour coding represents 
scenarios exceeding a set threshold: Green being counted within carrying capacity limits, light red represents 
an exceedance of the carrying capacity within 100-999%, dark red represent exceedance above 10,000% and 
the middle red coloured represents values in 1000-99.999% limit exceedance.  
 
                                                          
EE Representing the validation model, which was assessed/analysed in section Error! Reference source not found., and

From Table 35 it is seen that only one of the included impact categories, Ozone depletion, is not in the risk 
of being exceeded by the impact from the assessed cases with the current applied allocation. The utilisations 
are found as 1-4%, thus far away from the risk zone.  
For Marine Eutrophication it is seen that the Danish scenarios are within the carrying capacity allocated, and 
the exploitation is found to be within 20%-45%. 
 
By the general tendency in the exploitation of the carrying capacity allocated, is seen that the Australian 
scenarios are the ones which are further away from being able to be deemed absolute sustainable, as 4/7 of 
the exploitation factors are above 10,000%.

Page 49 of 85 
 Resulting impacts 
Figure 19 to Figure 24 shows the resulting impact of the main case scenarios as above, within the six impact 
categories assessed in this project, excluding the validation scenario. The results shown are found with the 
unit ‘impact unit/m2/yr.’. In a relative comparison, it is seen that the Australian scenarios have a higher 
impact than the Danish scenarios, except for ozone depletion, where Denmark is seen to have a higher 
impact. Furthermore, there is a pattern indicating that the modular scenarios (3-7b, 8c, 8d) have a lower 
resulting impact than the reference scenarios (1, 2, 8a, 8b). The exact values of the resulting impact for 
each scenario can be found in Appendix 32. 
 
 Life cycle stage contributions 
Considering the life cycle stages’ contributions, as shown for scenario 1-4 and 8a-8d in Figure 25 to Figure 32, 
it is seen that the operational energy is the biggest contributor. The remaining scenarios are likewise shown 
in Appendix 33. However, for the Danish scenarios, it is seen that the contribution from the operational 
energy is around 50% on average, whereas the operational energy for the Australian scenarios lies around 
80-90% on average. 
 
After the operational energy use, the second greatest contributing phase was found to be the production of 
materials, both original and maintenance. Transport was found to contribute between 1%-20%, often 
dominated by the applied worker transport. 
 
End of Life processes showed to contribute negatively, both maintenance and original material, varying 
between -1 to -10% across the scenarios. 
 
Considering the scenarios with changed material inputs, the change of insulation materials to respectively 
glass wool and PIR did not change the phase contributions significantly for the scenarios. Neither did the 
change of foundation system from concrete to steel screws, except a minor change in the productions for 
the Australian steel screw scenario. Considering the scenarios with changed materials inputs, the change of 
insulation materials to respectively glass wool and PIR, did not change the phase contributions significantly 
(operational energy still being dominant). Neither did the change of foundation system from concrete to steel 
screws, except a minor change in the productions for the Australian steel screw scenario. 
 
The results shown below are a total, considering aggregated results including all lifecycle stages and EoL 
treatment benefits. Due to modelling limitations, the disaggregation of e.g. recycling benefits has been 
impossible to perform, as some benefits are implemented in the used datasets, thus incurring 
beneficial/negative impact when characterising according to ILCD.  
 
When considering the extended life span in scenario 8a-8d (Figure 29 to Figure 32), a shift occurs where 
maintenance processes and operational use (mainly energy, as water still is below 1%), obtains larger 
contribution shares, as the amount of maintenance materials needed is increased along with the years of 
operation, incurring larger amounts of energy use.

 Building-component contribution 
Considering the building component contributions (which represents the embodied impacts), as shown for 
scenario 1-4 and 8a-8d in Figure 33 to Figure 40, it is seen that the installationsFF are the biggest contributor 
(contributing 40-75%), except for the Danish reference building, scenario 1 and 8a, where the floor finishing 
treatment is dominating in the category ozone depletion, which was found caused by the carpet/textile 
process applied. The remaining scenarios are likewise shown in Appendix 34. 
 
Next, after the installations, it was found that the contributions varied. For the Danish reference cases is was 
found that the outer walls, ground deck, outer walls completion (windows) and roof were contribution 
around 10-15%. For the Australian reference cases, the second biggest contributors were found as Ceiling 
completion, roof, outer walls and foundation, contributing around 20-35%. 
For the modular cases, the patterns were found somewhat similar, throughout Danish and Australian cases, 
making roofing finishing treatment the second biggest contributor, along with outer walls, and outer walls 
completion contributing around 10-25%. 
 
The results shown below are a total, considering aggregated results including all lifecycle stages and EoL 
treatment benefits. Due to modelling limitations, the disaggregation of e.g. recycling benefits has been 
impossible to perform, as some benefits are implemented in the used datasets, hence incurring 
beneficial/negative impact, of especially timber, when characterising according to ILCD. A somewhat 
aggregated distribution, dividing the modelled EoL phase from the rest, can be seen in Appendix 35.

4.3. Verification/validation  
The results of this section were found in order to check discrepancies from the coherent model against the 
study of (Brejnrod 2015). The input values have hence been based on input data and economic allocation of 
normalisation references in (Brejnrod 2015). The impact categories assessed are chosen to represent the 
ones assessed in this project and allocated as shown in Table 36.

When comparing the contribution from the different construction components, as shown in Figure 41, it is 
seen, that, except for the component ‘installations’, the contribution patterns shown are somewhat similar.

significantly higher than in the previous study, which was found to be caused by the carpet process.

It appears clear that the applied component groups vary from the study of (Brejnrod 2015) to the present 
study. This may incur some variations throughout the component distribution pattern. Furthermore, one 
important difference to note is the negative contributions in climate change, which in the present study are 
caused by the use of timber, e.g. in the roof. These do not occur in the study by (Brejnrod 2015), presumably 
as the impacts are characterised differently due to the use of ReCiPe methodology instead of ILCD.

in the referencing study, which lead to a range of modelling assumptions. These are listed and discussed 
further in Appendix 30.

4.4. Sensitivity analysis 
The sensitivity of inputs is checked on the four main case studies, scenario 1-4. The assessment results are 
shown in the noted Appendix, and main outcomes discussed below.

 LCI data validity 
As described in the literature and methodology section, the aim was to apply regional data to the greatest 
extent possible. However, when performing a sensitivity analysis between ecoinvent data and AusLCI data 
for the Australian scenarios, deviations were found which were assumed to be skewing the results to an 
extent where results would be incomparable. For this reason, the results shown above in all other sensitivity 
assessments than this section, have ecoinvent data applied for both the Danish and Australian scenarios, 
using European (RER) or swiss (CH) data for Denmark, and Rest of the World data (RoW) for Australia. 
 
As seen in Appendix 36, deviations for the Australian cases were found to vary from -97% to 8%, when using 
the ecoinvent-based results as reference, and from -7% to 3576%, when using the AusLCI based results as 
reference.   
 
These deviations in the results, along with scarcely explained knowledge of known flow issues, upon 
exporting AusLCI from SimaPro software into openLCA (mentioned by both openLCA operators and AusLCI, 
when obtaining the AusLCI data) was deemed too great an uncertainty in the modelling, and therefore the 
AusLCI results were opted out.  
 The AusLCI compatibility issue was additionally confirmed applicable for GaBi as well by personal 
communication with Ben Fisher, a sustainability engineer at thinkstep, confirming that no official document-
tation on the issue exists, however that a complete AusLCI database is currentlyGG not available due to 
compatibility issues. 
 
 Input value sensitivity 
The sensitivity of all inputs was checked with respectively 10% increase. This resulted in a matrix from which 
the calculation of a sensitivity ratio was found. This was found by applying equation (4). 
 
 
 
∆𝑖𝑛𝑝𝑢𝑡 (𝑖𝑛 %)
∆𝑜𝑢𝑡𝑝𝑢𝑡 (𝑖𝑛 %) = 𝑠𝑒𝑛𝑠𝑖𝑡𝑣𝑖𝑡𝑦 𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜 
(4) 
                                                          
GG Statement given on the 09.01.2018

From the sensitivity ratio is was then concluded whether a parameter was more or less sensitive, depending 
on

𝑠𝑒𝑛𝑠𝑖𝑡𝑣𝑖𝑡𝑦 𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜> 1,         𝑝𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑚𝑒𝑡𝑒𝑟 𝑚𝑜𝑟𝑒 𝑠𝑒𝑛𝑠𝑖𝑡𝑖𝑣𝑒 
 
The four standard cases, 1-4, were checked in this way.  
 
None of the modelled inputs were found to be exceeding 1, therefore they could all be said to be less 
sensitive. Some inputs turned out to be more sensitivity than others, listing the top 5 sensitive parameters in 
Table 40 to Table 42. All found sensitivity ratios can be seen in Appendix 37. For the two references cases, 
the sensitivity pattern top 5 showed similar, however with different ratio values. For the modular scenarios 
the top 5 sensitive parameter were found similar as well, except for EoL waste factor, being more sensitive 
in the Danish modular scenario. For the modular scenarios is was further found that the parameter 
‘installation’, was the 6th most sensitive parameter.

 Operational energy use 
The operational energy applied on the cases has, additionally to the input sensitivity above, also been 
checked for influence on case 1-4. This is done by respectively changing the operational energy valuesHH, 
and by removing the operational energy entirely, to see the performance of the building without it, and 
show the building material impacts. 
 
When changing the operational energy use, which in both countries resulted in a decrease, the impact results 
decreased as well, and the scenarios got closer to being deemed absolute sustainable. None of the scenarios 
however, stay within the carrying capacity limits for all impact categories, as seen in Table 43 and Table 44. 
                                                          
HH to respectively 37.7kWh/m2/yr. for the Danish scenarios and to 26.30 kWh/m2/yr. for the Australian scenarios. 
==PAGE:67==
 
Results 
 
Page 57 of 85 
The colour coding represents scenarios exceeding a set threshold: Green being counted within carrying 
capacity limits, light red represents an exceedance of the carrying capacity within 100-999%, dark red 
represent exceedance above 10,000% and the middle red coloured represents values in 1000-99.999% limit 
exceedance. 
 
By reducing the operational input to respectively regulatory limits for Denmark and empirical data for 
Australia, it shows that only the Australian reference now exceeds 10,000%. When removing the operational 
energy entirely, all four checked scenarios can be deemed sustainable, within the categories ‘Marine 
eutrophication’ and ‘Ozone depletion’. However, as no case maintains within the carrying capacity limit for 
all impact categories, no case can be deemed absolute sustainable.

4.4.3.1. Changed operational energy 
Looking at the phase distributions in Table 45, enlarged illustrative comparison with labels can be found in 
Appendix 38. For the scenarios, where the operational energy was reduced, it was found that the operational 
energy contributions decreased, leaving all other phases to increase their contribution. For the Danish 
scenarios, the operational energy decreased to less than 40% for every category except climate change. For 
those scenarios, the productions phases (both original and maintenance) thus increases to contributing more 
than 50%, and transport 10-20%.

to ~50-80%. The production phases thus gain and contribute ~20-35%.

From the assessment it is seen that the application of electricity does have an impact of the final 
environmental impact. This is mainly due to the very different energy source composition across countries.  
In the impact results, it is seen that the Danish electricity performs relatively better than the Australian in all 
categories assessed except ozone depletion, and that both the default electricity process for Denmark in 
ecoinvent and the modified process impact somewhat similar, except in ozone depletion, where the default 
impact almost twice as much. 
 
Considering the impact for electricity in NSW, extracted from AusLCI processes, they appear to impact less 
than the NSW process modified in ecoinvent, except in climate change. 
The Australian country processes from ecoinvent, seems to be somewhat similar across the impact 
categories, with the Australian default having a slightly larger impact, except in ozone depletion and 
photochemical ozone formation where it has a relatively smaller impact. The Australian country process 
extracted from AusLCI varies quite some across all the assess impact categories, except for climate change 
and marine eutrophication where they appear somewhat similar. 
 
 Allocation key variations 
As the economic allocation performed, varied between the countries, at test of the sensitivity of the applied 
allocation key was done. The Australian allocation of the carrying capacity/normalization reference, 5%, was 
applied to the Danish scenarios, and vice versa with the Danish allocation key of 13%.  
 
As seen in Table 47, the deviation follows the increase/decrease in the allocated % for a dwelling. By 
decreasing the available carrying capacity for the Danish scenarios, the exceedance of the limit is seen to 
increase, this, linear along with the allocated % change. For the Australian cases, when switching/increasing 
the share allocated to a dwelling, it is seen that the case studies come closer to the carrying capacity limits 
and thus being able to be deemed sustainable in the absolute term.  The Australian cases are however, even 
though the two allocation keys have been switched, still performing the worst/exceeding the limits by the 
most. 
 
Results for all scenario 1-8d, can be seen in Appendix 40, where the normalized results additionally are shown 
with colour coding as in the original results.

 Average number of residents 
As the number of residents varies across the countries, but for Denmark, also across the dwelling types, as 
test of the influence was made. For the Danish scenarios, the average number of residents, 2.1, was applied, 
which incurred a decrease of residents for the Danish reference building and an increase for the Danish 
modular cases. For the Australian scenarios, the number of residents was increased by 10%. The more 
residents, the more ‘available’ carrying capacity, thus larger normalization reference, per dwelling. 
 
As seen in Table 48, for all Australian scenarios and for the Danish modular design, the sensitivity test applied 
was an increase in the number of residents of the dwelling, which incurred a decrease in the impacts, and 
thus a movement closer towards the carrying capacity limits. For the Danish reference scenarios, the number 
of residents was decreased, and thus the results moved further away from complying with the carrying 
capacity allocated. The analysis was made maintaining the economic allocation factor for each country and 
the average floor area. It is seen that one solution to build more sustainable, would be densifying our living 
conditions (which is already seen applied in the modular designs, compared to the reference case studies). 
 
Results for all scenario 1-8d, can be seen in Appendix 41, where the normalized results additionally are shown 
with colour coding as in the original results. 
 
 Average dwelling area 
As the applied normalization references are adjusted to the unit /m2/yr., the average dwelling area, which is 
applied to obtain this, is tested for sensitivity by increasing the applied/used average floor area with 10%. 
The larger area, the less available ‘available’ carrying capacity, thus larger normalisation reference, per m2. 
 
In Table 49 it is seen that the impacts are increasing linearly with the increase of the floor areas, which incurs 
a smaller allocated carrying capacity per m2 dwelling, and hence an increased distance for the scenarios 
towards the absolute limit.

 Normalisation references applied 
4.4.8.1. Interchanging of EU/GLO normalisation references 
The sensitivity of regionality of the applied normalisation reference was checked by interchanging the 
references, while maintaining all other factors the same, and thus applying the global references on the 
Danish scenarios and the European references of the Australian scenarios. 
 
The global normalisation references are bigger than the European, due to a lower population density globally, 
for freshwater eutrophication, photochemical ozone formation and freshwater ecotoxicity. Marine 
eutrophication has a bigger reference for Europe than for the global, and ozone depletion along with climate 
change are similar for both Europe and global.  
 
When interchanging the applied normalisation reference, the Danish building thus gets closer towards the 
carrying capacity limits for three categories, and further away for marine eutrophication. For the Australian 
cases it is vice versa. Scenarios 1-4 are shown in Table 50. Regionalised normalisation references can thus be 
argued from this to be of importance, to reflect availability of resources/carrying capacity correctly.

4.4.8.2. Application of global ILCD normalisation references 
Due to characterisation compatibility limitations for the regionalised normalisation references found in 
(Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b), only impact categories compatible was chosen for this project. However, the 
global normalisation references, modified by  (Sala et al. 2016) to be compatible with the ILCD categories 
have been applied to see whether this may not be reasonable. All other factors in regard to allocation have 
been left untouched, thus only the normalisation reference is changed. 
 
==PAGE:73==

impact categories and coherent normalised results found in the project, the Danish scenarios vary (due to 
the shift from European references to global), and the category climate change for radiative forcing changes 
for the Australian scenarios as well, as only temperature reference is considered in the ILCD modification. A 
decrease in the normalised results is found due to an increase in the normalisation reference. See Appendix 
44 for values and deviations. 
 
When considering the importance of applying more than 'just' the chosen impact categories, it is seen that 
for the Danish scenarios the two additional categories: acidification and terrestrial eutrophication, are still 
within the carrying capacity threshold. Land use, however, exceeds the limits by being ~400% (less for the 
120-year span scenarios, but not below 100%). For the Australian scenarios, all three additional impact 
categories are exceeded, being 250% and up. The normalised results are shown in Table 51, additional 
categories available with (Sala et al. 2016) are highlighted with dark green. The colour coding represents 
scenarios exceeding a set threshold: Green being counted within carrying capacity limits, light red represents 
an exceedance of the carrying capacity within 100-999%, dark red represent exceedance above 10,000% and 
the middle red coloured represents values in 1000-99.999% limit exceedance. The actual values are found in 
Appendix 44.

Page 64 of 85 
5. Discussion 
5.1. Allocation of carrying capacity 
The allocation of the carrying capacity was based on various assumptions and decisions throughout the 
project. These can be said to be rather project specific, as they are based on the opinions and estimations of 
the author and supervisors and may change in other scenarios and contexts. Depending on your everyday 
perception, the opinion on what a ‘fair share’ is, may vary.  And hence no correct answer to this definition 
exists. 
 
By applying an economic allocation, detailed statistical data for both countries was found available, enabling 
a detailed allocation. However, again, the interpretation of these statistics may vary, as it can be argued 
which categories to include as a share to ‘the dwelling’’ and which may be allocated to other industries, 
products or services. The assessment of the two countries’ economic allocational share of the carrying 
capacity also showed to give a different outcome, having ‘only’ 5.7% of Australia’s GDP and 13.3% Denmark’s 
GDP allocated to the dwelling, which in turn has an impact on the final results. 
 
The application of economic allocation further assumes an equal allocation throughout all assessed impact 
categories, which may be argued to not be properly representable, as some industries may have larger 
impacts on some categories then other – an example being agriculture being expected to have a larger impact 
on eutrophication. 
 
Various allocation methods were considered, however the final methods was chosen to be economic, which 
is also the recommended baseline allocation method for most allocation scenarios in a detailed LCA,  given 
in the Dutch Handbook on LCA (Bruijn et al. 2002). 
 
The allocation was applied on normalisation references defined by (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) for respectively 
a global and a European perspective. The references are based on the assumptions that an equal share of 
the world’s capacity is given to each person (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b). While this, in the general 
consideration of the issue being a global responsibility and resource, may be argued to be fair, it could also 
be argued that other allocations were applicable. One method would be economic allocation, which however 
would tend to skew the resources and may result in developing countries, not being able to develop, due to 
them having a lower GDP then developed countries.  
 
An alternative way could be an inverted economic or market-share allocation. This would imply that the 
developed countries have already exceeded their limits, and thus need to reduce, whereas the developing 
countries would get some more freedom to act, in order to increase their standard of living. However, as 
mentioned earlier, no correct allocation methods have been defined, and will thus also differ depending on 
the person performing the assessment. A discussion on this matter could easily get a political and vulnerable 
topic. 
 
5.2. Model verification 
The model verification was performed to enlighten differences that may occur due to modelling, software, 
LCI datasets and conversion factor variances, influencing the output.  
 
The input of this model, scenario 5, was based on the data found in (Brejnrod 2015), to the extent possible. 
However, deviations of the magnitude between 42%-368% was found across the impact categories. 
Additionally, when looking into the building component contributions, some rather larges differences were 
found, in that, the present study/model found installations to be a rather significant contributor (>40%), 
==PAGE:75==
 
Discussion 
 
Page 65 of 85 
whereas in (Brejnrod 2015), it contributed <15%, across all impact categories. The known uncertainties of 
the verification modelling were also discussed in Appendix 30. 
 
As no correct answer exists, this verification test only showed that results to a great extent can vary, 
depending of the assumptions made in an/the present LCA, even though initial inventory appear the same. 
This further implies the great variations, and non-absoluteness in the determination of a product’s 
sustainability. And could allude other sensitivities and uncertainties in the comparison between different 
buildings, where inventory has been collated from various sources. It is however, based on the verification 
calculations, acknowledged that an issue in the model may occur, due to the significant change in 
contributions from installations. 
 
5.3. Environmental pressure 
The main project results, contained 16 scenarios, when not considering the verification, scenario 5. The 
inventory of the modelling was aimed to include as many phases and processes as possible, in order to 
approach the absolute impact and thus evaluate on the actual sustainability of the building. However, these 
widened inventory modelling, are to a great extent based on other studies are assumptions, and thus only 
very few empirical data. As this may increase the possibility of evaluating on the absolute impact, this may 
also have incurred great sensitivities and uncertainties, which however are touched on later, in section 5.5 
and 5.6. However, due to the fact that variations occurred in the verification scenario, although sought to 
align the system boundaries of the previous study, is must be stressed that impact seem highly dependent 
on presumptions made, and thus the resulting impacts found in the project may not be the ‘absolute 
impacts’. 
 
The scenarios assessed were based on 4 case studies.  
Scenario 1 and 8a, were based on a design, categorized to represent prevalent Danish construction methods 
and materials choices. Thus, the impacts found are presumed representable for a single family house, build 
according to the Danish building regulation (Trafikstyrelsen 2015). 
 
Scenario 2 and 8b, were based on a design found in the national standard, deeming it to be a ‘benchmark 
building type’ for a single story detached dwelling in Australia (National Standard Pty. Ltd. 2014), which must 
be interpreted as a representing standard building for current building practices in Australia.  
 
Scenario 3-7b and 8c-8d, were based on a design given by the modular building company, HusKoncept. The 
design was based on current Danish building regulations (Trafikstyrelsen 2015), and was designed by the 
company to represent a more sustainable solutions then the prevalent design. Is does thus represent a 
possible solution to lessen the environmental impact of the built environment. For the Australian scenarios 
hereof, a simplified modification was made, thus acknowledging the fact that the Australian modular design 
case, does not represent neither actual Australian building methods, nor a specific solution for the Australian 
building market, but a somewhat iterated solution.  
 
 Resulting impacts 
The immediate resulting impacts showed that the Australian scenarios have a bigger environmental impact 
across all categories, except ozone depletion.   
 
Furthermore, the scenarios based on the modular designed, showed to have an environmental impact (un-
normalised) which was less then what was found for the prevalent building designs of respectively Denmark 
and Australia.  It could thus be argued, that the proposed modular designs would be a solution towards an 
environmental less impacting single-family housing design. 
==PAGE:76==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 66 of 85 
 
 Absolute sustainability measures 
However, when normalising the impact according to the allocated normalisation reference based on the 
carrying capacity of the earth, none of the case studies are deemed absolute sustainable, i.e. complying the 
limit of a normalised impact of 100%.  
 
All the scenarios based on the two Danish cases, complied within both ‘Marine eutrophication’ and ‘ozone 
depletion’, whereas the scenarios based on the two Australian cases only complied the latter. None of these 
categories are approaching the boundary though, the closest for ‘Marine eutrophication’, being scenario 1, 
with 45% exploitation, and for ozone depletion, all results lying around 1-5%. 
 
Scenario 1, exceeded the remaining five assessed impact categories with between a factor 3.3-40, freshwater 
ecotoxicity being the lowest and climate change, radiative forcing the highest. When expanding the lifetime 
of the Danish reference case to 120 years, as done in scenario 8a, the exceedance factor was reduced to span 
between a factor 2.7-36. 
 
Scenario 2, was found to exceed the six remaining impact categories with a magnitude of 9-370, marine 
eutrophication being the lowest and radiative forcing for climate change as the largest as well. No significant 
reduction appeared when expanding the lifetime of the Australian reference case to 120 years, as done in 
scenario 8b. Though a reduction was found, the exceedance factor was still found within the same range 
(changing to 8.8-366). 
 
Scenario 3, found an exceedance factor, in the five remaining impact categories, between 2.4-27.5, with the 
same categories being the highest and lowest as in scenario 1. When expanding the lifetime, as done in 
scenario 8c, the exceedance factor was decreased to 1.6-14. The other scenarios based on the Danish 
modular case, scenario 6a.i, 6a.ii, 6b.i and 7a, all show the same patterns, with slight variations which are 
discussed later. 
 
Scenario 4 had an exceedance factor between 7-282 for the remaining six impact categories. The pattern of 
highest and lowest being the same as in scenario 2. Again, when increase the lifespan to 120 years, the 
exceedance decreases, but not significantly, compared to the general sizes of the impact limit exceedance 
(changed to a range of 6.9-282) 
 
Thus, none of the case studies, or scenarios assessed can be deemed absolute sustainable, with the best-
case scenario, considering the Danish modular case base input, would need a reduction between 60-96% 
throughout the various impact categories. For the best-case scenario, based on the Australia modular base 
case, a reduction between 86-99% would be needed to obtain absolute sustainability state. 60-96% 
throughout the various impact categories. For the best-case scenario, based on the Australia modular base 
case, a reduction between 86-99% would be needed to obtain absolute sustainability state.  
 
 Life cycle stage contributions 
When assessing the life cycle stages contributions, a general pattern showed that contributions share from 
the operational energy in the Australian scenarios were larger (mostly around 80-95%), whereas in the Danish 
scenarios the contributions was around 40-70%.  This increased for both countries, when increasing the 
lifetime to 120 years, however only slightly for the Australian scenarios. For all scenarios it was found the 
operational energy phase only contributed ≤50% in the category ozone depletion. 
 
==PAGE:77==
 
Discussion 
 
Page 67 of 85 
The varying resulting impact were, to a large extent, found caused by the differing operational energy 
requirements, as an analysis of the scenarios 1-4 without any operational energy, showed a more equal 
impact pattern. The Danish reference case in scenario 1 though stuck out for the categories ‘climate change’ 
and ‘ozone depletion’. 
 
Second next contributions life cycle phase varied slightly from scenario to scenarios, however production and 
maintenance production seemed to contribute somewhere around 15-50% across the Danish scenarios and 
impact categories, whereas contributing around 5-35% for the Australian scenarios.  
 
Though transport phases are often mentioned/concluded to be of smaller significance in the LCA of a 
building, the transport processes in the scenarios was found ranging between 5-20%, which is not deemed 
as insignificant. However, the borders of transport inclusions can sometimes be blurry. This project included 
an aggregated material transport, both for production, maintenance and EoL treatment, along with the 
transport of workers. All of which were based on assumptions, which could be argued too simplified, calling 
for a need to adjust these by empirical data (which would however require and actual working 
case/production line) and material specific supplier data, e.g. by using fleet route planning software.  
 
 Building component contributions 
The building component contribution distribution showed largely dominated by the installations in the 
building which again were driven by the applied ventilation unit. When considering previous studies, by e.g. 
(SBi 2015; Brejnrod 2015), installations were found to contribute in a range between 1-20%, for which reason 
a contribution share of 40-75% was found very suspicious.  
However, despite the model being re-evaluated and inputs tested for sensitivity, an explanation of this was 
not determined. It however suggests that the results assessed in this project may be skewed, and the actual 
contribution shares may vary in assessments done, if modelling installations differently. It must however also 
be noted that the final impact from the installations are still not the dominant impact sources, due to the 
phase of operation being found as the main impact contributor. This consideration is further applicable, when 
discussing the following contribution results. 
 
When disregarding the installations, it was found that the floor finishing treatment, driven by the carpets, 
were contributing ~80% in the category ‘ozone depletion’ for the scenarios based on case 1, the Danish 
prevalent building. In the other categories for scenarios 1 and 8a, the floor finishing treatment was found 
contributing 5-10%. In the other scenarios, across the countries, the floor finishing treatment as found to 
contributes <1%, mainly due to the main flooring material being timber, which is accounted negative 
attributes when using ILCD.  
 
The negative impact from timber, is also found showcased for the outer walls. For the scenarios based on the 
prevalent cases (both Danish and Australian), containing brick walls, the impact is seen contributing positively 
with 5-15% for all impact, however for the scenarios based on the modular design in both countries, the 
outer walls are seen to have a negative contribution of ~10% for climate change.  
 
For scenarios 2 and 8b, based on the Australian prevalent building design case, the use of gypsum 
plasterboard in the ceiling also to have large share, ranging between 5-10%. This, for all other scenarios is 
found <5%. As the inventory was given as a bill of quantities for the case (National Standard Pty. Ltd. 2014), 
the amount of gypsum was not estimated. However, the amount of gypsum used was found significantly 
higher for the Australian prevalent case (using 53kg plasterboard/m2 habitable floor area), than for any other 
of the cases (DK reference using 6.5kg plasterboard/m2 habitable floor area, and the modular cases using 25 
kg/m2). 
 
==PAGE:78==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 68 of 85 
The outer walls completions (windows) applied, varying in type, design and performance, showed to 
contribute equally, around 5-10%, across the Danish based scenarios, and for the Australian based scenarios 
around 2-3%.  
 
When discussing the above results, it must be held in mind that the results shown are aggregated, and thus 
not divided as recommended in (BS EN 15978 2011). This is seen as a significant limitation in the modelling 
of the results, as it is hard to derive which impacts are ’certain’ and which can be attributed to beneficial 
treatments thus avoiding production of next level materials. This limitation was found caused by several 
things: firstly, the software used, openLCA, was unable to show contribution layers further down than 4 tiers, 
leading to the next issue, being the modelling setup, that incurred more tiers than 4 available, for the modular 
scenarios, due to an extra production step when assembling the modulesJJ. This limitation of the contribution 
analysis may have been omitted by modelling in a more aggregated way, limiting the steps/tiers of modelling. 
Thirdly, and aggregation occurs when applying ILCD characterisation, incurring negative impacts of e.g. 
timber. As these flows are a part of the applied processes, this appears unaggregatable. Whilst the last ‘issue’ 
can be accommodated by using other characterisation methods, this would be up to the author/researcher 
discretion, and thus is a debatable decision like e.g. the applied allocations as well. 
 
5.4. Case scenario comparisons 
The discussion below is tried accommodating the various project description/hypothesis questions, given in 
section 1.1. As none of the assessed scenarios were found to be deemed absolute sustainable, the below 
discussion is based on a relative comparison, thus investigating how various applied options would affect the 
environmental impacts. 
 
 Comparison scenario 1-4 
Scenarios 1-4 represented the base case scenarios as found in the references. When comparing across the 
countries, it was found that the Australian scenarios performed worse than the Danish scenarios, mainly 
driven by the operational energy consumptions which varied from e.g. 71.75kWh/m2/yr. for the Danish 
references case, to 111.21 kWh/m2/yr. for the Australian reference case.  
 
When comparing the building designs, it was found that the modular building designs perform better, when 
comparing the Danish modular case to the Danish prevalent design case, and the Australian modular design 
with the Australian prevalent design case. 
The decrease, from prevalent design to modular design impacts, in the normalised impacts within the 
assessed impact categories that do not comply with the carrying capacity limits are shown in Table 52.

==PAGE:79==
 
Discussion 
 
Page 69 of 85 
 Comparison scenario 1-4 vs. 8a-8d 
The difference between scenario 1-4 and 8a-8d, were the applied lifespan of the building, being changed 
form 50 years to 120 years. A general finding was the impacts getting, relatively seen, smaller, due to the 
embodied impact (caused by e.g. transport, building structure and EoL phases) was distributed over a longer 
time span. As the operational energy was a static value /m2/yr., this increased linearly with the life span. This 
assumption of static energy use could be argued erroneous, because development in energy grid and 
technology in the building may change significantly over such a large lifespan. Even for a lifespan of 50 years, 
this argument could be applied, as much development is currently focusing on improving these aspects. 
 
As argued in a study reviewed by (Ghattas et al. 2016), use of a shorter lifetime may be the most relevant, in 
order to meet current climate change mitigation goals (Ghattas et al. 2016). In addition to this, it may be 
argued that, the longer the lifetimes applied, the larger uncertainties about processes, technologies and thus 
impacts. An example other than the energy sources, could be EoL treatment processes, which are currently 
developing and there is no way to know exactly how processes are applied in 50, nonetheless 120 years.  
 
It can also be discussed whether the applied lifetimes reflect the actual building lifetimes of the building 
designs and/or materials. An example is found for the average lifetime of an Australian house being 30 years 
(ReNew 2015), which would increase the resulting impacts due to less span distribution of the embodied 
impacts. 
 
 Modular design alternative 
To investigate possible influencing design decisions some ‘sub-scenarios’ (scenario 6a,i-7b) based on 
modified modular base cases were assessed.  
 
5.4.3.1. Material dependency 
Scenario 6a,i-6a,iv, assessed alternative insulations materials used for the outer walls, roof and decks. The 
results from 6a,I and 6a, iii, where the cellulosic insulation was replaced with glass wool in respectively 
Denmark and Australia, it was found that the resulting impacts where decreasing between 0-3%, see Table 
53. By a decrease in the resulting impacts, the normalised values thus also move closer towards the threshold 
value for being deemed sustainable in an absolute term. However not notably.  When changing the insulation 
to glass wool, the thickness of was maintained, however the density was found as 17kg/m3 instead of 
45kg/m3 for cellulosic insulation, thus the total mass of insulation needed decreased. 
 
Scenario 6a,ii and 6a, iv, assessed a situation where the cellulosic insulation was interchanged with PIR/PUR 
insulation foam. This showed to incur an increased resulting impact, see Table 53, across both countries, 
except for the categories ‘ozone depletion’ and ‘freshwater eutrophication’ in Australia. As the PIR insulation 
was found to have more efficient thermal properties, the thickness applied was reduced, along with having 
a density of 32kg/m3 thus having a smaller needed mass of insulation. Nevertheless, the impacts seem to not 
benefit from this.

However, other aspects may be considered than the final impact results. A steel screw system may e.g.

However, this outsourcing scenario was very simplified and may be argues to not covers all important actual
