Other, more practical aspects of outsourcing the production of modular buildings could be fact that transport 
of complete modular often incur increase cost for transportation and complex arrangements (Kamali & 
Hewage 2016) in regard to e.g. road accessibility and border clearance. Contradicting this argument though, 
it may be mentioned that containers of varying sizes are transported all over the world, which should be 
comparable to ‘boxed’ modules for housing. 
 
5.5. Sensitivity 
Throughout the project, various parameter, assumptions and decisions were stated to be subject for 
sensitivity analysis’. This was done, to define which decisions and assumptions would be more important to 
refine than others. However, it must to said that assumptions in general should be aimed avoided and 
replaced with actual measured or empirical data.  
==PAGE:81==
 
Discussion 
 
Page 71 of 85 
 
Some of the sensitivity checks made were done due to uncertainties in the information e.g. LCI data validity 
was checked. Other parameters were checked to see how they influenced the results output and thus the 
measure of the sustainability in an absolute term.  
 
The inputs were checked, to see whether these were more or less sensitivity. All the sensitivities were found 
below 1, however, the most sensitive input factor was found to consider the operational energy input. As the 
simulated energy uses in DesignBuilder were found ~twice as much as the prescribed values given in the 
Danish regulations for the Danish scenarios, all scenarios were modelled with either regulatory or empirical 
values.  
 
However, none of the applied variations helped the cases to be deemed sustainable in an absolute term. It 
helped highlighting some of the matters needed to be aware of when applying/performing similar 
assessments, and outline the understanding of some of the results variations when assessing the ‘absolute 
sustainability’ 
 
As mentioned earlier, the allocation is a quite subjective factor, for which reason all applied allocation input 
(allocation key, average number of residents, average dwelling area, applied normalisation reference) were 
tested for sensitivities.  
From the sensitivity analysis’ it was made clear, that not only did the Australian scenarios, in 
general/relatively, perform worse than the Danish scenarios, it also had some drawback regarding the 
allocation. Factors, which ‘helped’ increasing the normalised impact were a smaller economic allocation 
along with a larger average floor area, without the number of residents increasing significantly. 
Another sensitivity, or maybe an uncertainty, in the allocation for the Australian scenarios, is the fact that 
the Australian household-spending statistic was built on the HEC-system,  which again was based on the 
COICOP (Australian Bureau of Statistics 2012). The interpretations across these two systems may have 
influenced the final allocation key as well, due to differently categorised categories. 
 
A factor in regards to the allocated carrying capacity not further investigated, was the original allocated 
person—equivalent carrying capacity, allocated in (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b). As the study was done in 2015, 
the population number applied were data from 2010, however since then the population has grown, thus 
the allocated normalisation references would be assumed to decrease even further per person. The static 
nature of applying one population for allocation is a simplification, whereas they suggest accommodating a 
long-life product span by e.g. applying the forecasted population in the median year (Bjørn & Hauschild 
2015b). 
 
5.6. Uncertainties and limitations 
Assumptions made in the project were mainly done due to limitations or uncertainties of applicable data. 
This was caused partially by limited previous studies applying absolute measures, along with non-specific 
projects. Some of the main uncertainties found in this project are 
- 
Capacity estimations and normalisation references 
- 
Allocation of carrying capacity 
- 
Operational energy  
- 
Data availability 
- 
Tool 
 
==PAGE:82==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 72 of 85 
 Capacity estimations and normalisation references 
As discussed earlier, (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) themselves highlight some important uncertainties in the 
estimated carrying capacities and thus normalisation references. Uncertainties like simplifications of reality 
and  ambiguities on the definitions of carrying capacity, which by (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) is overcome by 
basing their references on scientific consensus. 
 
Additionally, as also discussed earlier, the characterisation methods used in (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b) a 
combination of various methods, due to marginal values. Even though modified version of the global 
references was developed by (Sala et al. 2016), the extent to which the references and applicability has been 
developed, is still limits thus incurring an uncertainty of the accuracy of the evaluated sustainability. 
 
From the sensitivity analysis it was found that not only did the scenarios exceed the 5/6 out of the 7 assessed 
impact categories, but when expanding the number of impacts categories assessed, normalised according to 
(Sala et al. 2016), the Australian scenarios exceeded all of them, and the Danish ones exceed one additional, 
land use.  
 
The discussion on applied impacts categories may however cause the need for a deeper analysis and 
discussion of which impacts categories are applicable and of importance and where, due the regional nature 
of some of them. This thus, also highlight the possible uncertainties by applying global, not national or 
regional, values for the countries assessed.  
 
(Evans & Ross 1998) argued some of the relevant impact categories for Australia and differences to Europe. 
Whereas Eutrophication, acidification and photochemical oxidants were found figuring strongly in the 
European LCA practitioner minds, they do not occur as significant in Australia. The difference of importance 
may, to some extent be explained by the rural nature of most of Australia, and thus photochemical ozone 
formation is found a problem in bigger cities like Melbourne and Sydney, whereas acidification in general 
was not found a problem in Australia (Evans & Ross 1998). 
 
The importance of applying correct references is also highlighted in the study providing the normalisation 
reference (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b). They mention the choice of reference region to depend on spatial 
extent of the impact category. Where categories like freshwater depletion and aquatic eutrophication ideally 
should be developed for regional territories, other categories like climate change and ozone depletion are 
argued to be a global dependant (Bjørn & Hauschild 2015b). 
 
 Allocation of carrying capacity 
As the allocation, as mentioned earlier, to a great extent is influenced by subjectivity, the methods for 
allocation is bound to uncertainties upon application. Further development of this would be beneficial, 
however, it may be argued not to be the job of engineers and researcher alone, but including social scientists, 
economists and politicians to reach a common consensus, that distributes the resources equally. 
 
 Operational energy  
As discussed in the sensitivity section above, the modelled operational energy consumption in DesignBuilder 
were found ~twice as much as the Danish regulatory values. Another uncertainty additional to the modelling, 
is the fact stated about lack of commissioning, thus control of the actual energy consumption for both 
countries (Wittrup & Holm 2006; Dansk Energy Management & Esbensen A/S 2015; Christensen & Malmskov 
2017; ArchitectureAU n.d.; Jewell 2015; Energy Australia 2010). Research performed by (Kumar et al. 2017). 
For Denmark, it was found that the average exceedance is around 20-25%, with some case hitting a high of 
50% transgression (Wittrup & Holm 2006). 
==PAGE:83==
 
Discussion 
 
Page 73 of 85 
 
The operational energy consumptions applied further only contain regulated energy consumptions, thus 
excluding consumptions from e.g. lighting and appliances. In order to assess the actual sustainability in an 
absolute it would be argued that these should be included as well. 
 
 Data availability 
As discussed earlier, the aim for applying regional and national data was not applicable due to compatibility 
issues with the Australia LCI database. This thus implies uncertainties on the result outcome being proper 
representable when using ecoinvent RoW data.  
 
 Tool and LCIA method 
As previously discussed, the use of OpenLCA implied some limitations upon results extraction, disabling 
proper sub-component analysis when too many tiers in the model appeared. Further, from previous studies, 
it is known that differences occur across used LCA modelling software tools (Silva et al. 2017).  
 
By using ILCD as the LCIA methodology, some aggregations were implied, amongst other timber being 
accounted for with beneficial properties in the category climate changes, which seems not to appear, when 
applying e.g. ReCiPe. This thus implies an uncertainty of the result interpretations, as the results may look 
different when using another characterization methodology.  
 
==PAGE:84==
Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts 
 
Page 74 of 85 
6. Conclusion 
Throughout the project, various conclusions have been made. The overall conclusion on the project research 
questions/hypotheses were found but some additional qualitative and quantitative conclusions were drawn 
during the project.  
 
 Building scale 
Considering the first research questions, asking if the suggestion of the Danish buildings not being sustainable 
in (Brejnrod et al. 2017; Brejnrod 2015), a similar conclusion was drawn, thus not being able to deem the 
Danish prevalent building design sustainable in an absolute  term, considering the carrying capacity of the 
earth, transgressing 5 out of 7 assessed impact categories. It was further found that it is still predominently 
operational emissions that contribute to the impacts, despite high building regulations, but that materials 
play a significant role as well, with between 20 – 60%. Thus, the state of not being deemed sustainable is still 
largely driven by the operational phase, as the impact across countries and materials appear more alike for 
embodied impacts.  
 
When assessing the Australian prevalent building design case, it was found that the Australian design 
exceeded the carrying capacities by even bigger factors for 6 out of the 7 assessed impact categories, thus 
implying it to be even less sustainable.  
When considering the embodied impacts of the two prevalent building designs, a somewhat equal impact 
value was found. Thus, it may be concluded that the fact that the buildings cannot be deemed sustainable is 
not caused by specific materials for specific application in Nordic countries.  
The large difference in resulting impacts was found to be caused to a higher operational energy consumption 
for the Australian prevalent buildings than what was found for the Danish prevalent building. The distribution 
between operational and embodied impacts is relatively found to be closer to 60/40 in the Danish prevalent 
building design, and 90/10 for the Australian prevalent building design. 
 
On the second part, suggesting a modular based construction/design would increase the sustainability-
performance, it was found that this is true. However, not to an extent where the building can be deemed 
sustainable in an absolute term. The same conclusion was found throughout all modular design variations, 
none of them being deemed absolute sustainable.  
However, as for the prevalent cases, it was found that the Danish building designs performed better that the 
Australian.  
 
However, the due to the large uncertainties found throughout the project, it must be stressed that this study 
only represents one way of assessing the sustainability of a building, along with the results being case specific 
and thus non-applicable for general conclusions within the most sustainable way to build.  
 
 Methodological 
The results and conclusions were, however, influenced by some limitations. From this it has been concluded 
that while awareness about considering the planetary boundaries is rising, the methods for coupling this to 
environmental impacts are still not well-developed. 
 
The applied allocation, both for the normalisations references, but also the economic household/dwelling 
allocation performed in this project, are not developed far enough to be the base of a certain analysis. 
 
Regarding available LCI data, it was found that data was available in theory, but not in practise. This leads to 
a conclusion of urging the need of development. Even though technology and pre-sets continuously develop 
which may make it hard to keep up, there is a need for the data be equally continuously developed in order 
==PAGE:85==
 
Conclusion 
 
Page 75 of 85 
to perform actual, and absolute, assessments of the environmental impact caused by buildings (or other 
products and services). 
 
Further, it was found that the basis for a comparison across two countries was rather scarce and difficulties 
arose, implying great uncertainties on the comparison baseline. Some of the issues were found to be 
caused by e.g. different operational energy regulations/thresholds, varying legislations and differing 
cultural habits. 
 
The project was surely limited by the time frame available and would to great extent win from being 
performed by a multidisciplinary team (structural, energy, sustainability etc.), applying interdisciplinary 
knowledge and thus increasing the quality of input, thus output, of the environmental impact of a building. 
 
6.2. Recommendations and future research 
This project is just a starting point/initiative to consider evaluating the sustainability of our buildings in an 
absolute term – there is a long way before usable comparisons may be available.  
 
Some future aspects which are worth considering and developing are: 
- 
Data development 
- 
Database interoperability development 
- 
Expanded research and development of assumptions and actual case/scenario data 
 
Additionally, it was found that not many LCA assessments on Australian residential building were available. 
Many assessments either concern commercial building or ‘only’ concern carbon or energy assessments. For 
Denmark availability was assumed reflected in European studies. However, the suggestions of further and 
extensive development of local or regional LCI data is strongly encouraged, as this is needed to improve the 
environmental impact assessments performed throughout all industries.

Appendix 1– Official building regulations 
 
Page 1 of 136 
Appendix 1– Official building regulations 
Denmark 
‘Bygningsreglementet’ is the Danish building regulation. The latest updated version is Building Regulations 
2015 (Trafikstyrelsen 2015). The regulations are based on functional requirements and covers both 
construction of private and commercial buildings of all sizes, putting out instructions within layout, 
administrative regulations, structural, fire safety, indoor climate, energy use, and installations. It is 
administered by the Danish Transport and Construction Agency and follows the Danish Building Act. Some of 
the main regulations, related to this LCA project, for single-family houses, are listed in Table 1. 
 
Australia 
The National Construction Code (NCC) is a performance-based code developed by initiative of the Council of 
Australian Governments (COAG) and maintained by the Australian Building Codes Board (ABCB). To comply 
with the NCC, a building design/solution must comply with the ‘Performance Requirements’. (Australian 
Building Codes Board 2016a; Master Builders 2016). The code contains of three volumes, which each covers 
respectively: 
- 
Volume 1: multi-residential, commercial, public and industrial buildings and structures 
- 
Volume 2: residential and non-habitable buildings and structures 
- 
Volume 3: plumbing and drainage 
 
The code sets out minimum requirements regarding health, safety, sustainability and amenity of construction 
and design of new buildings, nationwide in Australia. Each territory or state is then responsible for 
administering the codes and some states have additional guidelines (Australian Building Codes Board 2016a). 
Some of the sections of the NCC have specified or modified versions for the states or territories. As this project 
is assessing residential single-family housing, only NCC Volume 2 will be examined according to main 
requirements related to this LCA project. The items can be seen in Table 2. The type of houses assessed fall 
within building classification 1a. 
 
The section about energy efficiency in NCC, section 2.6 and 3.12, based on NatHERSA, is waived in the state 
New South Wales (NSW), due to the implementation of BASIX. Measures are hence changed to complement 
and support BASIX in NSW. The values and requirements for energy given in Table 2 are the general NCC 
regulations. They are given for comparative reasons between regulations in Denmark and Australia. 
 
State specific – New South Wales 
As the project concerns a site-specific assessment located in the Australian state; New South Wales, specific 
requirements from the state government has been examined as well. The regulations are additional to the 
NCC, and it is made very clear upon assessing NSW legislation that to be approved, the/a building project must 
comply with the Building Code Australia (BCA)/NCC. The energy assessments and regulations are set through 
BASIX, which is outlined in the main report and explained in Appendix 2 – Energy use assessment methods. 
The main regulations and acts given by the NSW government for the construction industry are  
- 
the ’Environmental Planning and Assessment Act 1979’ (NSW Government 2017b) 
- 
the ’Environmental Planning and Assessment Regulations 2000’ (NSW Government 2017c) 
- 
the ’Home Building Act 1989’ (NSW Government 2017d) and the ’Home Building Regulation 2014’ 
(NSW Government 2017e) 
 
                                                          
A Energy rating system

Appendix 2 – Energy use assessment methods 
Denmark 
The energy requirements for buildings in Denmark are set out in the building regulations (Trafikstyrelsen 2015). 
The regulations, in addition to putting up requirements regarding building element performance, put out 
energy performance limits, which are updated every couple of years, to reduce the environmental impact 
through energy usage. By the current regulation and limits set, called BR15, a house may not exceed an energy 
use (for heating, ventilation, cooling and hot water) of 30 kWh/m2.year along with an additional 1000 
kWh/year throughout the entire heated floor area. This limit is calculated as shown in equation (1). 
 
 
 
𝐸= (30 + 1000
𝐴
) 
(1) 
 
Where  
𝐸=  𝐸𝑛𝑒𝑟𝑔𝑦 𝑐𝑜𝑛𝑠𝑢𝑚𝑝𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 𝑎𝑙𝑙𝑜𝑤𝑒𝑑, 𝑔𝑖𝑣𝑒𝑛 𝑖𝑛 𝑡ℎ𝑒 𝑢𝑛𝑖𝑡𝑘𝑊ℎ
𝑚2 /𝑦𝑒𝑎𝑟 
𝐴=  𝑡ℎ𝑒 ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑑 𝑓𝑙𝑜𝑜𝑟 𝑎𝑟𝑒𝑎, 𝑔𝑖𝑣𝑒𝑛 𝑖𝑛 𝑚2 
 
For buildings aimed to reach outstanding performance currently, there is a class called ‘energy performance 
2020’. This puts out a total performance requirements for energy use of 20 kWh/m2.year (Trafikstyrelsen 
2015). This will be introduced as the applicable limits in the future. 
 
To assess and calculate these requirements, a tool called BE15H is developed by the Danish Building Research 
Institute. BE15 is given along with SBi-instruction 213 (Aggerholm & Grau 2016), and a licence is needed to use 
the tool. The calculations are done in a user interface which leads the user through the essential inputs to 
calculate the energy performance of the current design at hand.  
 
The input phases and information’s needed are listed in Table 3. In addition to these, basic information like 
floorage, construction site, climate data, and orientation need to be registered. The output is given in key 
figures like total energy consumption in kWh/m2.yr., and compliance with either performance requirement 
2015 or 2020, along with information about contributions to energy demand, net demand, heat loss from 
installations and selected electricity demands.  
 
The input phases and information’s needed for BE15 are listed in Table 3. In addition to these, basic 
information like floorage, construction site, climate data, and orientation need to be registered. 
 
Australia 
Australia has developed several tools to reduce the GHG-emissions through increasing the energy efficiency of 
the buildings designed. On national plan, two main tools have been developed: The Nationwide House Energy 
Rating Scheme (NatHERS) and the National Australian Built Environment Rating System (NABERS), of which the 
former is implemented as NCC requirements. In NSW, another tool has been developed to assess energy 
efficiency in the state, namely the Building Sustainability Index (BASIX). BASIX is mandatory to apply in NSW 
and replaces NatHERS requirements from NCC. 
 
                                                          
H BE15 is the current and latest update. Earlier the tool has been called BE10, reflecting the Building Regulation version 
of the time.

NatHERS  
NatHERS  is a rating system for residential buildings in Australia and is administered by the Department of 
Environment and Energy/Commonwealth Government, on behalf of all Australian state and territories 
Governments (Department of the Environment and Energy 2017) 
 
The rating system is based on a star rating, with a best score at 10 stars. To pass and thereby be compliant 
with the NCC requirements, a minimum score of 6 stars is required, and 10 stars represent a design where 
neither heating or cooling is needed to obtain comfortable thermal indoor climate. The rating is based on 
building materials and climate zones. Appliances are not included, and hence energy used by lights, household 
appliances and hot water systems are not accounted for by using NatHERS (Department of the Environment 
and Energy 2017). Some of the main features of NatHERS are shown in Table 4. 
 
To apply NatHERS rating to a housing design, a certified assessor is required. They’ll use the software 
developed and information such as: specifications and sizes of openings, sizes and functions of rooms, 
construction type, orientation of dwelling, location, and building materials, windows, and products are put into 
the program. The results are held against the range of comfortable indoor temperatures, and calculate the 
heating and cooling needed to outweigh the periods outside this range. The software applies the latent heat 
energy from occupants and heat from cooking, electrical appliances and lighting, which affects the heating and 
cooling needs of the building (Department of the Environment and Energy 2017) 
 
As NatHERS is compulsory through NCC which is legislation throughout entire AustraliaJ, the climate zones in 
which NatHERS ‘operates’ are divided into 69 zones. The climate is based on average data, compiled for at 
least 25 years, from the Bureau of Meteorology (BoM), and include data like humidity, solar radiation, air 
temperatures, and wind direction and speed.  
 
NABERS  
NABERS is a rating system which measures environmental performance of homes, buildings and tenancies. It 
measures water usage, energy efficiency, indoor environment quality and waste management by assessing 
performance data, e.g. utility bills, and transforms the performance results into a star rating from 1-6, 6 being 
the best rating to achieve (Office of Environment and Heritage n.d.) 
NABERS is managed by NSW Office of Environment and Heritage on behalf of the Commonwealth Government 
and the other state and territory governments.  
NABERS works as a voluntary rating system which enables building owners, managers or occupants to compare 
environmental impacts of their building to other buildings, and from that improving the performance of the 
building. 
 
The rating system contains of four separate tools for each of the sections; water, energy, waste management 
and indoor environment. These tools help measure the actual performance of a building once operation has 
commenced, and it is therefore not for use during the design stage of a building, hence it is after construction, 
whereas NatHERS is pre-construction. The system can, opposite NatHERS, be used for commercial buildings 
such as offices, hotels and shopping malls (Office of Environment and Heritage n.d.).  
 
NABERS is not compulsory, however some sectors put out requirements to improve the performance of 
buildings. Governmental instances renting office spaces e.g., have a minimum requirement of a 4.5-star 
NABERS rating in their tenancies. Some of the main features of NABERS are shown in Table 4, and compared 
to key features of NatHERS. 
 
                                                          
J Except in NSW, where NatHERS is replaced by BASIX

BASIX 
BASIX (Building Sustainability Index) (NSW Government n.d.) has been implemented throughout NSW through 
the ‘Environmental Planning and Assessment Act’ (NSW Government 2017b). To file a development/building 
application in the Australian state NSW, a BASIX assessment and certificate must be included. BASIX was 
introduced through the NSW planning system in 2004 and aim towards reducing the potable water 
consumption along with the ghg-emissions (NSW Government n.d.) 
 
BASIX assesses a proposed design against some pre-set targets within EnergyL and Water to enhance the 
sustainability of the outcome. The targets are defined based on a benchmark across the state. The NSW 
benchmark is a constant value; however, the targets are diverse throughout the state, considering factors like 
varying local conditions e.g.: different climates and housing types. The benchmarks are based on average NSW 
annual potable water consumption or ghg-emissions from the residential sector on a per capita basis (NSW 
Government n.d.). The benchmarks for NSW can be seen in Table 5. 
 
TABLE 5 – NSW BENCHMARK VALUES FOR POTABLE WATER CONSUMPTION AND GHG-EMISSIONS (NSW GOVERNMENT N.D.) 
 
 NSW benchmark 
POTABLE WATER CONSUMPTION (WATER CONSUMPTION)  90,340 L water/person/yr. 
GHG-EMISSIONS (ENERGY CONSUMPTION)  3,292 kg CO2/person/yr.  
 
The BASIX targets are varied and tend to decrease the further rural the area gets. Sydney is located along the 
NSW coastline, and the given targets, for a detached and semi-detached dwellings, are found as seen in Table 
6 in (NSW Government 2017a; NSW Government n.d.). 
 
TABLE 6 – BASIX ENERGY AND WATER TARGETS FOR A DETACHED DWELLING IN SYDNEY (NSW GOVERNMENT 2017A; NSW GOVERNMENT N.D.) 
 
Target zone Target reduction 
ENERGY TARGET  E1A 
50 % 
WATER TARGET  
40 %

==PAGE:106==
Appendix for: ”Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:

Page 8 of 136 
 
 
 
Along with the set targets, BASIX also sets out minimum performance levels for thermal comfort, measured as 
annual amount of energy used for heating and cooling in a dwellingM (NSW Government n.d.) 
 
In order to assess/apply BASIX, a checklist is formed, to have all input for the assessment (NSW Department of 
Planning and Environment 2017). For a single dwelling, some of the inputs are listed as: Fixtures (measured in 
rating from 1-6 stars), water supply systems, construction technical details (e.g. floorage, construction type, 
wall construction details, roof/ceiling construction details, insulation, window specifications), energy supply, 
ventilation, lighting supply.  
 
Monitoring showed that for assessed BASIX certificated single dwellings  between 2004-2007, studied 2007-
2009, the median reduction in ghg-emission was found to 13-22% (Energy Australia 2010). Research performed 
by (Kumar et al. 2017) found actual performance results of BASIX rated building to be varying as well.

Appendix 4 – Other rating systems in the Built Environment 
Denmark 
 
TABLE 7 – LIST OF COMMON SUSTAINABILITY LABELLING FOR BUILDINGS IN DENMARK FROM AUTHORS KNOWLEDGE AND (HANSEN 2017; ULLUM ET AL. 2013) 
DGNB 
It is worth noticing that DGNB is a holistic tool, assessing not only environmental 
sustainability, but also social and economic aspects, following the triple bottom line 
method. Furthermore, they add in considerations about technical- and process 
quality (DK-GBC n.d.), and newly they’ve added a new tier which is currently in the 
pilot stages, called ‘diamond certification’, which assesses the architectural quality  
(DK-GBC n.d.). 
Svanemærket/the 
Nordic eco-label 
The label is known in the Nordic countries by many consumers through products 
available in supermarkets and alike. This is seen as a strength and one of the assumed 
reasons why this marking is applied to the building industry.  The aim of the Nordic 
eco-label is to reduce the total environmental strain from consumptions. 
In order to obtain the label, the project has to comply with requirements to, amongst 
other energy consumption, materials which are harmful to environment and health 
of residents, and comprehensive documentation controls (Miljømærkning Danmark 
2017). 
 
The marking is spread across all Nordic countries, and in Denmark alone, the number 
of labelled housesN are 360 which are finished, and more than 400 which are currently 
in tender and construction (Miljømærkning Danmark 2017) 
Cradle to Cradle 
certification 
The certification is an international initiative, which labels all types of non-food 
products.  Manufacturers can get their products cradle-to-cradle certified, which 
counts as assurance that the material is approved within requirements for material 
health, material reutilisation, renewable energy, water stewardship and social 
fairness (Vugge til Vugge ApS n.d.) 
By choosing products for the building design which are cradle-to-cradle certified, the 
sustainability of the building is enhanced. 
LCAbyg 
A tool developed by SBi, financially supported by the Danish Transport, Construction 
and Housing Authority.  It’s developed to help get an initial idea of the environmental 
impacts and use of resources of a building design. It is designed to cover the entire 
building life cycle and is an open source tool (Trafik og Byggestyrelsen n.d.) 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                          
N Apartments, single family houses, terraced houses and child care centres

Appendix 4 – Other rating systems in the Built Environment 
 
Page 11 of 136 
 
Australia 
 
TABLE 8 – LIST OF COMMON SUSTAINABILITY LABELLING FOR BUILDINGS IN AUSTRALIA FROM AUTHORS KNOWLEDGE AND (GETTING TO SUSTAINABILITY 2012) 
Green Star 
Green Star assesses life cycle assessments in various ways, which give credits in the 
total rating. Either a material’s life cycle impact as holistic view, or an environment 
product declaration are ways of obtaining credits (Milagre 2015) 
Green Star is, like DGNB a holistic rating tool, considering the triple bottom line 
definitions by rating, and further covers aspects like Communities, Construction, 
Interior and Performance (Green Building Council of Australia 2015) 
GBCA additional 
tools 
The GBCA has, additionally to Green Star some other rating schemes that are material 
or component specific (Green Building Council Australia 2017). 
- 
Good Environmental Choice Australia (GECA) 
- 
Carpet Institute of Australia Environmental Certification Scheme (ECS) 
- 
Australasian Furnishing Research and Development Institute (AFRDI) 
- 
Sustainable Materials Rating Technology (SmaRT) 
- 
GreenTag GreenRate 
Most of these tools assess building interiors e.g. furniture, flooring, assemblies 
(Green Building Council Australia 2017). 
E-tool 
e-tool is an online Life Cycle Design app which is applicable for most types of 
construction projects, being either single or multi-family residential, commercial 
development or infrastructure.It is designed to comply with EN15978 and ISO 14044, 
and works on an open use-basis (eTool 2015a) 
 
eTool was originally developed in Australia, but has spread to other parts of the 
world. It complies with many of the major rating tools for sustainability in the built 
environment, and is hence not a separate rating tool (eTool 2015b). 
EnviroDevelopment 
Is a certification scheme reviewing projects of developmental format on aspects like: 
Waste, Energy, Materials, Community, Ecosystems and Water (EnviroDevelopment 
2011a) 
It is developed to ease the choice for purchasers, by labelling products or 
developments that have been rated friendlier to the environment by 
EnviroDevelopment (EnviroDevelopment 2011b). 
NSDO Ecolabelling 
Ecolabelling developed with standards within construction and building materials in 
order to give an opportunity for producers and manufacturers to show the 
environmental performance of their products. This is published trough a range of 
standards and baseline assessments (National Standards Development Organisation 
n.d.) 
Passivhaus Australia 
Provides various services within optimising building, covering all types from 
residential to commercial. A certification is obtainable which ensures that a building 
has been designed to maintain pleasant indoor temperature – all year round – along 
still being very low energy buildings, with minor energy consumption for cooling or 
heating (Passivhaus Australia n.d.)

Appendix 5 – General availability of LCI data 
As this study is to perform an LCA in both Australia and Denmark, it was chosen to investigate the LCI dataO 
available, as the author mainly has been working with European data until now. As many papers have been 
assessing this topic, both availability and how to address lack of availability, this review mainly assessed papers 
addressing the established and available databases, which could provide an idea of possible databases to use 
in this project. 
 
A report on Activity of Task Force 1 by (Curran 2006) was aiming to sum up available LCI databases, globally, 
and some interconnected LCA software, used in Canada, Latin America, USA, Asia, APEC region, Africa and 
Europe. The study found action throughout all continents, however of various extent.  
 
From assessing various papers, a list of available and existing databases is shown in Table 9 based on (Curran 
2006; Valdivia 2016; Khasreen et al. 2009; Soust-Verdaguer et al. 2016; Islam et al. 2015; Minkov et al. 2016; 
Greenhouse Gas Protocol n.d.). As the list has been assembled from various papers, it is assumed to include 
most existing databases, however it is not guaranteed that it is exhaustive. The databases are both on 
national/regional databases and databases developed by industry organisations. As this review however only 
aims to establish an idea and find relevant LCI databases for the project, all databases have not been 
thoroughly checked and some of them may hence not be available anymore.  
 
In an assessment looking at principles for collection of comprehensive and consistent data, focusing on 
Oceania, (Kellenberger 2015) found that there is a lack of appropriate data in this area in general. New Zealand 
has no database and hence no specific NZ data available. Further he stated to experience that many 
professionals working with LCAs do not find it appropriate to use data from overseas, as this may not reflect 
on current practise. He emphasises the importance of listening to this and developing the data available to be 
consistent. 
(Peters 2009) wrote an article concerning the availability and future development of LCI data in Australia. He 
found that the LCA society mainly were focusing on developing databases already established, by renewing 
data, standardise the methodologies and improve the processes.

==PAGE:115==
 
Appendix 6 – Uncertainties highlighted in review-studies 
 
Page 17 of 136 
Appendix 6 – Uncertainties highlighted in review-studies 
(Ghattas et al. 2016) mention some of the uncertainties in some of the reviewed studies and methodologies. 
Amongst other they mention the actual lack of accounting uncertainty as a severe problem. Further they 
mention the availability of data, where many of the studies had used commercial data instead of primary data, 
i.e. from on-site. They also highlight the life times assumed in the project as a major uncertainty, due to the 
natural dynamics of development, resulting in an unknown future about e.g. electricity supply. A change over 
time of electricity supply may lead to a lower impact contribution from energy use, shifting impact patterns. 
 
Specifically the impact of the difference of electricity grid was also highlighted in (Buyle et al. 2013). One of 
their reviewed studies compared a dwelling in Spain and Colombia. As 20% of the energy required in the study 
is covered by electricity, the way of producing this, obviously has an impact of the environmental impact results 
of the cases/scenarios.  
Also (Ramesh et al. 2010) touched on primary sources of energy. They mentioned the location and the 
conversion efficiency of materials production properties as a crucial factor, having great influence on the 
impact results. Their example on this is in the change from a fossil fuelled energy source to renewables, where 
the environmental impact decreases significantly.  
 
Uncertainties have also been addressed in the review by (Buyle et al. 2013). They divide methods of uncertainty 
investigation into four categories: 
1. Stochastic errors and variability of values describing output and inputs which are caused by things like 
temporal variations, measurement uncertainties and process specific variations. 
2. Data appropriateness 
3. Uncertainty of the model which could be caused by e.g. inappropriate process descriptions 
4. Exclusion/neglection of important flows 
 
They conclude that even though the first category of uncertainty investigations is rather easy to perform due 
to sufficiently available data, only one of their 38 reviewed studies actually assessed it. The other three 
categories they admit to be hard to calculate wherefore they suggest they be accommodated by gathering 
additional and more precise data (Buyle et al. 2013).  
(Soust-Verdaguer et al. 2016) highlights the use-phase of LCA modelling to be of high uncertainty, mainly due 
to lack of data availability, and hence most studies based their cases on estimations. 
 
(Soust-Verdaguer et al. 2016) also highlights the choice of database, and potential mixing of databases, as an 
uncertainty to be aware of. One of their reviewed studies compares impact results from various databases, 
and whereas their tendencies of the results showed somewhat similar, the numerical values varied between 
the databases.

Appendix 8 – Database presentation 
 
Page 19 of 136 
Appendix 8 – Database presentation 
 
Ecoinvent 3.3 
Ecoinvent 3.3 (ecoinvent 2016)  is one of the newest versionsT of the European developed database ecoinvent 
and was launched in 2016. Ecoinvent has been working within developing datasets for more than 20 years, 
and contains more than 13.300 datasets for LCI within the categories: Energy supply, Agriculture, Transport, 
Biofuels & Biomaterials, Bulk & specialty Chemicals, Construction materials, Packaging materials, Metal, Wood, 
and Waste treatment (ecoinvent n.d.).  
Datasets in ecoinvent are mainly based on European data, but are moving towards containing datasets from 
other parts of the world, like e.g. US. Most of the data have origin in Switzerland (CH), but also 
common/average European (RER), Global (GLO) and Rest of the world (RoW) datasets are developed. 
 
The ecoinvent datasets are further used as background database for several other tools. It contains  data in 
both unit process and system process level (ecoinvent n.d.).  
 
The database supports several methodologies, e.g. CML 2001, ReCiPe, IMPACT 2002+ and ILCD. The indicators 
covered are e.g.: Fossil depletion, Land formation, (urban and natural) Ozone depletion, human toxicity, 
Particulate matter formation, ionising radiation, marine ecotoxicity, freshwater ecotoxicity, Metal depletion, 
terrestrial ecotoxicity, eutrophication (freshwater and marine), agricultural land occupation, Photochemical 
oxidant formation, terrestrial acidification, and climate change. 
 
AusLCI 
AusLCI is the Australian National Life Cycle Inventory Database, and is developed by Australian Life Cycle 
Assessment Society (ALCAS). The aim of the database is to provide an easy-accessible, public , national  
database which contains transparent, comprehensive and authorities environmental data on various  
Australian products and and services. The database is based on ISO 14040 (ALCAS 2011). 
 
The database currently contains more than 460 datasets and holds datasets within Agriculture, Transport, 
Chemicals, Electricity, Fuels, Materials, Waste Treatment and Water. The data varies between being individual 
unit processes and cradle-to-gate processes. 
 
The database has been developed over the last decade with contributions from several stakeholders, e.g. the 
Australian Government, Australian Research and Development Corporations and Research Agencies.  
The data in AusLCI are reviewed at a technical level which is to ensure that to data provide a good 
representation . Further they have recently begun to review them at a compliance level, to ensure the data 
are within standard – this is however an on-going process (ALCAS 2011).  
 
The database aims to cover major impact categories and hence cover: Resource Depletion (fossil fuels and 
minerals), Water use, Climate Change, Ozone Layer depletion, Acidification, Eutrophication, Photochemical 
oxidant formation, Human toxicity, Eco-toxicity and Land occupation (Aus LCI committee 2014). 
 
 
 
 
                                                          
T Ecoinvent 3.4 was released in october 2017, but has not been obtained when working in this project.

Page 20 of 136 
Appendix 9 – Impact categories assessed  
Definitions and short explanations on the included impact categories, on which the performance of the case 
studies are evaluated primarily based on (European Commission Joint Research Centre 2010).  
 
Freshwater ecotoxicity [PAF]*m3*day 
Ecotoxicity measures the relative risks and associated consequences from chemicals released to the 
environment, accounting for the chemical’s destiny in the environment, hereunder exposure to species and 
reposes hereof in toxicological mattes.  When measuring ecotoxicity, most often acute  and local effect are 
omitted, and only long-term risks are included. 
 
Climate change [kg CO2 eq.] 
Climate change is a measure that accounts for the green house gases emitted by man made activity. 
Greenhouse gasses are gases that have radiative forcing properties. When gasses are emitted to the 
atmosphere, they allow the short hat waves from the sun to get into the atmosphere, but the long waved 
radiation from earth is kept inside the atmosphere and hence heating it up. 
 
The radiative forcing varies throughout greenhouse gas types, and a common group name is hence often 
referred to as Global Warming Potential (GWP). Whether it is listed as GWP or Climate Change, the unit is 
mostly transferred or given as kg CO2-eq. Other greenhouse gases are hence converted into their impact 
measured in CO2. And example is Methane, that has a conversion factor of 25 (US EPA n.d.). 
 
Ozone depletion [kg R-11 eq.] 
Ozone depletion is a measure to account if the equal balance of ozone production and destruction is off. 
Whereas it is a natural continuously process, caused by the sunlight and chemical reactions, the depletions 
occurs when the ozone deconstruction dominant caused by free radical catalysts in the form of chlorine, nitric 
oxide and atomic bromine (cholorflourocarbons).  
 
When this depletion occurs, the risk of harm to both human, flora and fauna appears, due to increased 
exposure to solar ultraviolet UV-B radiation. This can results in skin cancer, suppression of immune systems, 
premature aging along with damaged aquatic ecosystems and terrestrial plant life.  The impact on ozone 
depletion of often measured in either kg R-11-eq. or kg CFC-11-eq. (Chloro-Fluro-Carbon), which are the same. 
However, other compounds may be added to the group, applying a conversion factor. 
 
Eutrophication 
Eutrophication, that being freshwater, marine or terrestrial, measures the impact on flora and fauna through 
the macro-nutrients phosphorus and nitrogen.  By increase of the nutrients in the nature, the plants are given 
new conditions for growing, changing the ecosystem. When more nutrinet occur, speciace that benefit from 
high levels of nutrient may be favourised, and the nature hence changes, which affect the fauna as well and 
new living condition occur. 
 
Aquatic eutrophication is most measured by it’s limiting macronutrients, limiting the growth of algae. Most 
often phosphurus is seen to be the limiting nutrient in freshwater systems whereas nitrogen is the limiting 
nutrient in marine systems. The aquatic systems are exposed to waterborne emissions, being either phosphate 
and other phosphorus compounds (measured as a total P), or nitrate and other nitrogen compounds 
(measured as a total N). If the body of water is very large, aquatic system may by exposed by airborne NOx-
emissions. 
 
==PAGE:119==
 
Appendix 9 – Impact categories assessed 
 
Page 21 of 136 
Photochemical ozone formation [kg NMVOC eq.] 
Photochemical ozone formations measures pollutants in the air, which are extremely reactive and oxidises 
organic molecules when getting in contact.  It is produced near the surface of the earth and is sometimes 
referred to as smog.  
 
Photochemical ozone formation influences humans when it gets in contact with the respiratory systems, 
through inhalations and can affect the flora by attacking the surface of the plants. When the ozone, along with 
other reactive oxygen compounds, is inhaled by humans, it comes in contact with the surfaces which causes 
respiratory diseases and tissue damage. The unit of photochemical ozone formation is mostly given in Non-
Methane Volatile Organic Compounds (NMVOC) or ethylene equivalents (C2H4-eq) and are generated by 
incomplete combustion and evaporation. 26% of NMVOC emitted by combustion processes are e.g. emitted 
by road vehicles and other transport sources (Hjelgaard 2017).

Appendix 15 – Assumptions: Windows/door 
The prevalent Danish design provides a mass of materials (glazing, aluminium and wood) in (SBi 2015a; 
Brejnrod 2015), whereas the two other cases supplied window information in m2 of windows. To convert these 
dimensions into masses or volume, a frame to glass ratio was found by assessing reference window designs, 
which are listed in Table 22. 
 
The windows in the Australian prevalent design have single glazing. The glass ratio is assumed similar to double 
glazed alu/alu panes (Newman Replacement Windows 2014) and is found as shown in Table 22. 
 
For the modular Danish design, windows of the type VELFAC 200 Energy are projected, which are found with 
a glass to framing ratio of 84% glass (VELFAC A/S 2016a; VELFAC A/S 2016b). The glass to framing ratio of the 
windows assessed in the Australian fitted modular design is assumed as double glazed alu/wood windows. The 
roof lighting in the modular design is assumed similar in both the Danish and Australian scenario.  
 
With the values given in Table 22, the masses of glass were found by multiplying the area of glazing with total 
thickness of glass in each window type, e.g. for a 3-layered window the thickness was found as 3*4mm=12mm.

By knowing the total mass of glass used in the window as explained above, the total mass of the window 
materials were found by applying the percentage-wise distribution found in (Owsianiak et al. 2017). With this 
total window mass, a mass of the rest of the window component materials were then found. A calculation 
example is shown below: 
 
STEP 1 Area of window: 1m2 
Glass percentage in window: 84% 
 
Area of glazing in window: 0.84*1m2=0.84m2  
 
 
STEP 2 Total glazing thickness:2*4mm=8mm=0.008m 
Density of glass: 2600kg/m3 
 
Total mass of glass in window: 0.84m*0.008m*2600kg/m3=16.8kg 
 
 
STEP 3 Mass wise percentage of glass in window: 73.19% 
 
Total mass of window: 16.8kg/0.7319=22.95kg 
 
 
STEP 4 Apply percentage wise divisions of every other materials in window. E.g. wood 
Percentage of wood: 12.2% 
Mass of wood in window: 0.122*22.95kg=2.80kg 
 
Material distributions for 3-layered windows are based on the VELFAC 200 Energy design (VELFAC A/S n.d.), 
used in the modular Danish base design, and distributions are shown in Table 24. The material distribution of 
the roof lighting was found from an environmental product assessment of a Velux roof lighting (VELUX 2013) 
and is shown in Table 25. 
 
The thickness and mass of the aluminium frame in the prevalent Australian building is based on (Daming 
Aluminum Window n.d.), assuming a sheet thickness of 1.4mm (found in the aluminium profile page) and a 
need of 3 ‘layers’, based on drawing 6/7 in the profile drawing.  
 
                                                          
Y Others beeing mentioned as Acrylic binder, Triethylene glycol and wood perservative. Assumed neglectable due to small 
amounts/contributions  
Z Others beeing mentioned as Argon. Assumed neglectable due to small amounts/contributions

Appendix 19 – Waste treatment practices 
 
Page 47 of 136 
Appendix 19 – Waste treatment practices 
Waste treatment practices in Denmark 
Waste statistics 
The Waste Statistics (Toft et al. 2017) from DenmarkCC, gathered from 2013-2015, showed in simplified 
numbers that in 2015 87% of the primary waste generated within construction and demolition waste was 
recycled, 6% was landfilled and 6% was incinerated. Soil was not included in these statistics, but reported 
separately. 1% was found as special treatment waste, (Toft et al. 2017). 
 
The paper divides the statistics into 8 main waste sources. The ‘Building and construction’ source, contributing 
with 37% of the total waste in 2015DD, contains, amongst others, the ‘construction and demolition waste’ 
category (Toft et al. 2017).  
 
The category is defined to contain bricks, concrete and tiles, amongst other. It is however mentioned, that not 
all construction and demolition waste may have been accounted for in the category, as it could also fall under 
other categories such as: glass, waste suitable for incineration, wood, waste suitable for landfill, PVC, 
impregnated wood , and plaster (Toft et al. 2017). 
 
Waste treatment procedures 
Even though (Toft et al. 2017) stated that 87% of construction and demolition waste was recycled, a distinction 
and definition of recycling must be made. Hence, many studies consider ‘downcycling’ as a part of recycling, 
which is backed by the fact found in (SBi 2015b), that most waste within the construction of demolition industry 
is crushed and used for road filling. A little part is put into landfill or incinerated (SBi 2015b). The patterns and 
treatment are however dependent on material types to be demolished. 
 
Bricks are, by some companies, reused directly. This is done by cleaning the brick for mortar after 
demolishment. The cleaning is done either mechanically or manually. By reusing the bricks in this way, the 
energy needed for new brick production can be saved, however a mechanical cleanse will still require some 
energy used (SBi 2015b).  If not reused directly, brick and other similar materials like e.g. concrete and tiles, 
are crushed and put into road fill, which replaces the need for extracting new gravel and sand (SBi 2015b).  
 
Gypsum plasterboards are, in Denmark, mainly produced from left over gypsum materials, which occurs during 
the cleaning process with sulphur. This residual product is used to produce gypsum/plaster boards, and in that 
way, natural gypsum resources are not extracted in order to produce this product (SBi 2015b). This way of 
handling waste products saw its first well-functioning example in a Danish city called Kalundborg, and is called 
Industrial symbiosis (Ellen MacArthur Foundation 2017). This was also where the method with gypsum was 
established between an energy producer and gypsum manufacturer (SBi 2015b).  
 
Some sorts of insulation is/can be produced of recycled materials. One of the major Danish rock wool 
manufacturers allows customers or/and demolition companies, to return their used insulation. This will then 
be used for production of new insulations materials (Rockwool A/S 2017b). To ensure that the quality of the 
material does not decline, the company is reviewed and controlled by third parties, and they are only able to 
reuse ‘clean’ insulation – hence no insulation with aluminium foil or other things (SBi 2015b).  
 
                                                          
CC Based on ADS. ADS= AffaldsDataSystem, translated into ”Waste Data System”. It’s a database which enables collection 
of waste flows in Denmark (Miljøstyrelsen n.d.). 
DD Without considering the soil, which is reported seperately. If included, the quantity of the waste is estimated to  
increase to more than double the current/reported values for the industry (Toft et al. 2017) 
==PAGE:146==
Appendix for: ”Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts” 
 
Page 48 of 136 
 
Glass wool is another type of insulation which contains primarily of reused materials. A company claims to 
produce their glass wool insulation of 80% recycled glass. The last 20%, of the total 95% glass used for glass 
wool insulation production, are from newly produced glass. This process is also verified by third party 
companies (ISOVER 2017; SBi 2015b). 
 
Waste treatment practices in Australia 
Waste statistics  
In the year 2014/2015, Australia generated about 20 million tonnes of construction and demolition waste. Of 
this waste, it was found that approximately 64% was recycled and 35% was landfilled (Reardon et al. 2013). 
 
A study into construction and demolition waste in 2000-2005, identifies the distribution of typical construction 
materials as shown in Figure 2based on (Reardon et al. 2013). 
 
 
FIGURE 2 – CONSTRUCTION MATERIAL DISTRIBUTION OF WEIGHT OF TOTAL CONSTRUCTION AND DEMOLITION WASTE IN AUSTRALIA 2014/2015 (REARDON ET AL. 
2013) 
 
Waste treatment procedures 
In Australia, a national waste policy was published in 2009 (Australian Government n.d.), which also includes 
a specific strategy for construction and demolition waste.  
 
The concept of industrial symbiosis has also gained interest in Australia, and an organisation, governmentally 
supported, was established in 2009 called the Australasia Industrial Ecology Network (Edge Environment Pty. 
Ltd. 2012). The purpose of the organisation is to promote the awareness and opportunities.

Appendix 19 – Waste treatment practices 
 
Page 49 of 136 
 
A report on construction and demolition waste, designed as a guide, was released by (Edge Environment Pty. 
Ltd. 2012), and performed on behalf of/for the Australian government. In this guide, major construction 
materials are examined in regard to their recycling and re-use possibilities.  
 
Bricks and concrete are summed as one product, as these often occur in one mixed ‘bowl’ being processed 
equally. They are often used as aggregates in low-grade roads and for sub-bases of pavements, substituting 
virgin gravel or crushed rocks (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012; City of Whittlesea 2016). 
 
Considering the metal recovered from construction and demolition, most of it is often steel (~95%), and other 
are usually copper, stainless steel and aluminium (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012), which all have a high 
recycling rate. 
 
Timber is usually not a material recovered directly and to a large extend when considering construction and 
demolition waste.  However, hard-wood is considered a high-value market,  if it can be properly recovered 
from a building site, where they are used in landscaping projects or new constructions (Edge Environment Pty. 
Ltd. 2012). However, some companies in Australia specialise in recycling timber, reprocessing into e.g. wood 
chips, MDF and particleboards (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012). 
 
Plasterboards are a material of high recycling potentials, however they often get lost in mixed C&D loads, 
which makes recovery minimal. A practical separation is mentioned as being slowed/minimised by the 
mechanical demolition processes, mostly taking place due to time pressure, safety and occupational health , 
and applies to most of the recovery processes of C&D wastes (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012). 
Some companies within the plasterboard industry have worked towards separating the boards directly, and 
sorting agreements are made between on-site contractors and Gyprock, enabling a 100% recyclability. When 
recycling gypsum plasterboards, the materials processed are implemented into the new productions. 
Complications of recycling however arises, when contaminations by other building materials occurs, like e.g. 
metal fixings, chemicals and general waste (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012). 
 
A case study assessed in (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012), looks at the Old Leura Dairy, where 80-95% was 
constructed of recycled and re-used materials like framing timber, floorboards, corrugated iron sheets, sinks, 
toilets, windows, doors, bricks, light fittings and carpets. The project however was both more expensive and 
labour intensive than if equal virgin materials had been used (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012). From the study 
it is further mentioned that professional use of reclaimed materials may often be even more complicated due 
to adaption, knowledge and experience needs. 
 
Another case study highlights the potential for recycling of EPS waffle pods used in foundations. Clean 
materials can be assorted at site from off-cuts, and enables granulation and recycling into 40% of new waffle 
pods. However, as with plasterboards, once contaminated with other construction materials this recycling is 
being ruled out (Edge Environment Pty. Ltd. 2012). 
 
Glass, if separated from drinking bottles, can be used as aggregates in concrete once cut, or be remelted in 
some situations (Reardon et al. 2013).

Appendix for: ”Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts” 
 
Page 50 of 136 
Appendix 20 – EoL treatment rates applied 
 
 
Table 32 summarises recycling rates and incineration percentages applied for treatments or materials, were 
they are respectively applicable. The amount/material not considered recycled and/or incinerated, are 
assumed into landfill. 
 
Even though plasterboard was found to be 100% recyclable (GypsumRecyclingInternational n.d.; REGYP - 
Recycling Solutions & Gypsum Sales n.d.), arguments were also found that in order for this process to take 
place, the product needs to be clean and cannot be contaminated by other constructions materials. A common 
assumption is hence made based on recycling rates for Denmark (Dall et al. 2003). 
 
Even though glass/glazing was found to be recyclable, though in various rates, (Hyder Consulting et al. 2011; 
GlassForEurope n.d.), no adequate datasets were found for modelling this process. Glazing is hence assumed 
100% landfilled in this project. 
 
Incineration of waste was found to be a rather rare practice in Australia (Kane 2015; Lowrey 2017; Vinyl Council 
of Australia n.d.), which was supported by the lack of incineration topics for products in Australia. 
 
Table 32 provides a recycling rate for concrete, and other masonry materials. From this recycling rate, a 
replacement of newly produced gravel is performed in the modelling. A discussion is not covered by this 
project, whether the process performed with concrete as ‘recycling’ should be listed as recycling, or if a new 
process should be developed for ‘downcycled’ materials, which still work to reduce production of new 
materials, but by downcycling, the chance of another next-service life, is reduced. 
 
Recycling rates were found in literature for plastics, however not all processes were available as LCI processes. 
The modelling has hence been divided done by applying other plastics products within the same EoL treatment, 
e.g. the mixed plastics are modelled with polyethylene processes. Further, as only Australian recycling rates 
were found for plastics, the European data are applied to Australia scenarios. 
 
PVC is assumed treatead with both recycling, incineration ant landfill, according to shown references in Table 
32. However, it must be mentioned that, especially incineration, is not necessarily considered the best 
solutions due to poor energy generation abilities and emissions of CO2, toxic substances like dioxins, and by-
products, many of these carcinogens, such as vinyl chloride, chlorobenzene, polychlorinated Biphenyl’s (PCBs) 
and aromatic hydrocarbons like toluene, naphthalene and benzene (Greenpeace International 2003). 
 
The bitumen roofing on the modular buildings is assumed 100% incinerated in both countries, due to limited 
LCI data availability. The calorific value is set as 40.95 MJ/kg (Abbasov et al. 2008). 
 
Paint is assumed to be disposed like the surface treatment below (respectively gypsum plasterboard and glass 
felt wall covering), hence the treatment is modelled like 100% landfill. 
 
Even though some landfill depots are designed to extract energy (Land Fill Gas Industries n.d.; US EPA 2017; 
LMS Energy 2017), the energy utilisation of this has not been accounted for in this project.

==PAGE:153==
 
Appendix 22 – Assumptions: Material wastage/spill 
 
Page 55 of 136 
Appendix 22 – Assumptions: Material wastage/spill 
 
The spill of materials throughout the lifecycle has been estimated as seen in Table 34.  
(Quale et al. 2012) stated that anecdotal evidence showed on-site contractors to usually order 5-15% surplus 
materials.  This number may vary depending on materials, however, it is in this study assumed constant at 5% 
for the construction phase of the conventional case studies.  
 
For the modular building, a waste factor of the construction phase of 3% is assumed, based on estimation 
performed by HusKoncept, and additionally from the modular building source in (Kim 2008). 
 
As maintenance procedures are assumed more accurate and specific, a waste factor of 1% is assumed for both 
modular and conventional case studies. 
 
For both scenarios, a 5% reduction/waste in avoided material production is further applied on recycling and 
incineration. This means, if e.g. 100kg concrete is sent for recycling, ‘only’ 95kg avoided gravel is applied, as 
5kg are assumed wasted/misplaced. As the utilisation rate would naturally differ from material to materials, 
and the energy recovery from incineration as well, this assumption is check for input sensitivity. 
 
For verification purposes, the waste fraction is assumed similar to (Brejnrod 2015) in order to ease comparison 
and results verification.  Here 1% spill is assumed for construction and maintenance phase, and a recycling 
wastage of 5%. 
 
TABLE 34 – MATERIAL SPILL ESTIMATIONS FOR SCENARIOS 
LIFE CYCLE PHASE 
SPILL 
 Conventional design Modular design 
CONSTRUCTION 5% 
3% 
MAINTENANCE (USE PHASE)PP 1% 
1% 
RECYCLING (EOL PHASE) 5% 
5% 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                          
PP Maintenance of exposed and replaced material throughout the use phase. Materials as roof tiles, plasterboards etc.

Appendix for: ”Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts” 
 
Page 56 of 136 
Appendix 23 – Assumptions: Transport 
Material transport and site location 
The transport of respectively materials and modules is assumed done on an average large cargo lorry, suitable 
for 16-32 metrics tons load. The assumed locations throughout the buildings’ life cycles are shown in Table 35 
and the average distances between stages of the life cycles are shown in Table 36. 
 
The two construction methods and sites are assumed to be built by professional builders with large scale/lots 
procurement contracts. Hence the supplier for both construction methods, conventional and modular, are 
assumed from material manufacturer level. However, optimisation is assumed, why the materials are 
transported/’bought’ from the nearest distributor. 
 
As transport distances appear to be rather subjective, due to various distributor decisions, which will possibly 
vary from project to project, the sensitivity of the input is checked when modelling the LCA. Distances should 
however not be applied to other studies, as the ones assessed are project specific assumptions and locations. 
 
Danish location of production and site are based on conversations with HusKoncept about their estimated 
customer segment placements and production facilities. Distributors are defined as the nearest saw mill 
production of respectively Herning and Aarhus, to represent distributors/manufacturer. EoL treatment 
location is defined as the nearest treatments/recycling centre, as further knowledge about landfill stations was 
not known.  
 
For Australian cases, the location of site has been based on an eastern suburb of Sydney (according to the 
construction code’s climate zone 5). The production site was based on a modular housing company from 
Australia, designing homesQQ. Distributors are defined as the nearest saw mill production of respectively 
Riverstone and Sutherland, to represent distributors/manufacturer. EoL treatment location is defined as the 
nearest waste management centre, as further knowledge about landfill stations was not known.

Appendix 23 – Assumptions: Transport 
 
Page 57 of 136 
 
 
Alternative distances for outsourcing scenarios of modular buildings 
As mentioned earlier, a case scenario for the modular housing looks at effects from potential outsourcing of 
production of modules.  The modified locations and distances are shown in Table 37. As only the production is 
outsourced, the sites and EoL treatment locations are assumed the same.  
 
The outsourcing scenarios assume that the outsourcing will be done ‘locally’ and thus for the Danish scenario 
the outsourcing is done to Eastern Europe and for the Australian scenario the outsourcing is done to China.  
When assessing transportation which includes outsourcing, the transport to Denmark is expected done with 
lorry, through Europe, and the transport from China to Australia is assumed done by lorry and ship.

8,500km (ship) 
 
Assessing outsourcing in this project only looks at the impacts transportation may have on the final 
environmental impacts, as outsourcing of production may bring various other implications, some of which can 
be mentioned as import/export legislation, border control and politics. These are however not discussed 
further in this project. 
 
Worker transport 
The transport of the workers is included as well in this study. The transportation is assumed in 
person/passenger vehicles, one person per vehicle. As no specific data have been obtainable assumptions are 
made towards the need of workforce. 
 
A working year is assumed as 48 weeks, of 5 working days each=240 days. A month is assumed 4 weeks. 
 
After conversation and assessment of calculation papers with HusKoncept, an estimation was done, that 
approx. 12 workers were needed for the production, with a productivity of 120 modules 60m2 per year. As 12 
modules are needed in a cluster of the modular design (6 units), the total time for a cluster would hence be 
assumed as 24 daysSS.  
 
Based on (Kim 2008), an assumption of on-site workforce needed is set as 8-10 people. The conventional 
building time is assumed 3.5 months, and the modular design is estimated to use 3 weeks on-site work 
additionally to the 24 module production days (Kim 2008). 
 
According to (Quale et al. 2012), an uncertainty factor of 50% is added to the total conventional on-site 
transport need (in tkm), due to surveyed uncertainty of scheduling delays where workers may show up to find 
that the building is not ready for their work. As the modular building is built in controlled conditions, this 
uncertainty is assumed neglectable. 
                                                          
SS This is seen as a conservative estimation, as one of the benefits of modular production is parallel production and hence 
production time would be minimised.

Appendix for: ”Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:  
A Comparison between Danish and Australian Contexts” 
 
Page 58 of 136 
 
 
 
As no specific location of worker homes is defined, an average location is assumed for respectively 
conventional and modular housing. An average commute time in a car is assumed to approx. 1 hour, covering 
an average distance om 70km. The author is aware of the uncertainty of this estimation, due to necessity of 
commute flexibility and distances normally commuted. However, an assumption must be made in order to 
assess the cases. The input is checked against sensitivity.

Appendix 24 – Operational energy decisions and modelling 
 
Page 59 of 136 
Appendix 24 – Operational energy decisions and modelling 
 
As the case studies were found from various references, operational energy has been assessed via simulation. 
The Australian reference building did not have any specific energy use data, but prepared the ground to, more 
or less, perform energy simulations. The danish reference case, and the modular design, were stated to stay 
within the danish regulation energy frame BR15. 
 
In order to align operational energy baseline, DesignBuilder (DesignBuilder Software Ltd n.d.) has been used 
to model the buildings. Modelling settings and decisions can be seen in Appendix 25 – DesignBuilder Modelling. 
As the modelling in DesignBuilder was done based on scarce design information and details, the operational 
phase input into the LCA modelling has been exposed to a sensitivity analysis.  
 
The distribution of energy consumption on respectively heat and electricity, has been based on (Marsh et al. 
2008), accounting 65% of the total primary energy use for electricity, and 35% for heat for Denmark. For 
Australia, all energy is assumd sourced by electricity based on (Energy Rating 2015), determining that most 
energy needed for operational heating, cooling, lighting and Hot water is provided by electricity. From this, 
and the modelling in DesignBuilder, the operational energy for the building are modelled as shown in Table 
39. 
 
The operational energy applied has only been from the buildings. Contribution from e.g. urban infrastructure 
and transport energy og occupants has not been included. 
 
TABLE 39 – OPERATIONAL ENERGY AND WATER USE FOR THE 4 CASE SCENARIOS IN [KWH/M2/YR]

Uncertainties 
- 
HVAC is very loosely chosen due to insufficient data sources and minimal knowledge of systems applied 
o This may be accommodated in further studies by implementing researchers with extensive 
experience within energy modelling 
- 
Australian scenarios are based on article/case study of new Australian apartments HVAC needs 
(DAIKIN commercial 2017) 
- 
Danish designs are based on DesignBuilder template for Denmark 
o Due to lack of deep knowledge and explanations on HVAC systems in domestic buildings, this 
decision has been done. 
o Danish energy modelling is often done in the program BE15. In this program the energy 
modelling is done separately for ventilation, lighting, mechanical cooling, hot domestic water, 
with specific appliance settings. As the case scenarios are not know in this detail, coarse 
estimations have been necessary due to shortage of time for this project and energy modelling 
not being the main purpose of the project

General uncertainties 
The output areas did not comply entirely with the theoretical floorage. The total energy use was thus divided 
by the DesignBuilder floorage below, which resultet in the operational energy consumptions as given in the 
main report. 
 
1. DK reference: 127.37 m2 
2. AU reference: 230.82 m2 (As the garage was included for modelling)  
3. DK modular: 94.61 m2 
4. AU modular: 101.91 m2 
 
Further, the input in DesignBuilder were all modelled as electricity. However, the results in th eoutput given 
where divided into various categories, e.g. district heating, cooling, electricity etc. This was found due to a 
compatibility/calculation difference between DesignBuilder and EnergyPlus. Once you have selected 
"electricity from grid", the results displayed to agree with those calculated by EnergyPlus (simulation engine 
of DB) directly for district heating and cooling. In other words, there is a difference in the way DB and E+ work 
that causes a problem here. This is also explained by:  
 
”DesignBuilder applies a fuel type and a CoP to the idealised heating and cooling 
systems used in Simple HVAC, whereas EnergyPlus displays the unprocessed 
loads under 'District heating'. This is because the idealised systems in EnergyPlus 
are considered to be met directly without use of HVAC equipment through a district 
heating/cooling type system.” (DesignBuilder n.d.) 
The only way to cope with this issue would be to change the type of HVAC from simple to "compact". In the 
recent version of DB this is named "detailed". However, as the modelling was done in a trial version, this was 
not available.

Appendix 26 – Assumptions: Demolition energy 
 
Demolition energy is often neglected under the assumption that the value is small enough to not matter (Dixit 
2017). However, as this project assesses the absolute sustainability, the demolition energy has been included, 
based on previous studies’ assumptions. (Dixit 2017) found that on average in reviewed studies, the demolition 
energy accounted for 3% of the total embodied energy. 
 
From previous literature various demolition energy values were used, however mostly along with a note of 
great uncertainty and lack of actual data. The demolition energy consumptions found are listed in Table 47, 
assuming the energy source is electricity due to lack of precise data. 
 
TABLE 47 – DEMOLITION ENERGY USED IN OTHER/SIMILAR STUDIES [KWH/M2] 
Reference study 
ENERGY USE 
Building type assessed 
(Gustavsson et al. 2010) 
10 
Eight stories, wood framed apartment building, SE 
(Dodoo et al. 2011) 
5 
Four stories, wood frames apartment building, SE 
(Winistorfer et al. 2005) 
8.4-9.6BBB 
Single family houses, 1-2 stories, US 
(Adalberth 2000; Adalberth 1997)  
~3-10 
Single unit dwellings, 1 story, SE 
(Guan et al. 2015) 
9.72 
Light construction (e.g. wood frame) 
29.44 
Medium construction (e.g. steel frame) 
48.89 
Heavy construction [e.g. masonry, concrete) 
 
Based on the above previous studies, the demolition energy is set according to (Guan et al. 2015), assuming 
the modular constructions are within light construction and the prevalent building are heavy, due to being of 
masonry. The values are listed in Table 48. The demolition primary energy is covering disassembly, transport 
and recovery. The energy consumption for demolition is assumed per m2 habitable floor area. 
 
TABLE 48 – DEMOLITION ENERGY VALUES

= 777.78-888.89 kWh per 1000 square feet, where 1000 square feet=92.90m2.

Appendix 27 – Allocation of carrying capacity 
 
As the used databases/statistics, base their definitions of household categories on the ‘Classification of 
Individual Consumption According to Purpose’ (COICOP) categories defined by the United Nations Statistics 
Division, a short unravelling of the system is done below. The system will hence also be used for the allocation 
methods in this project.  
 
The COICOP system contains of 14 top level categories (United Nations 2017), as shown in Table 49. 
 
TABLE 49 – COICOP CATEGORIES (OECD 2016) 
CP01 – Food and non-alcoholic beverages 
CP08 – Communication

other fuels

routine household maintenance

CP06 – Health

institutions serving households (NPISHs) 
CP07 – Transport 
CP14 – Individual consumption expenditure of general 
government 
 
For this project, as wanting to allocate to a dwelling, only some of the categories are deemed relevant, namely 
CP04 and CP05.  
 
Economic allocation 
The economic allocation is based on household consumptions, it will hence result in one allocation key to apply 
on all impact categories.  
 
The household spending of respectively Denmark and Australia, were found as a percentage of the total Gross 
Domestic product (GDP) in the OECD database for household spending (OECD 2016). The spending from 2010-
2016 are shown in Table 50.

Appendix for: ”Global Environmental Assessments of Modular Building Concepts:

Page 72 of 136 
From these divisions and sub-divisions, the allocation factor can now be found. It is important to note that the 
allocation performed is subjective in the form that it is based on the categories deemed relevant by the author, 
and other categories may be assumed relevant by others. 
 
The shares are multiplied up throughout the distributions, and hence for category 04, the total share is found 
as: 
27% + 36% + 5% ∗37% + 9% ∗25% + 22% ∗((34% ∗40%) + 9% + 4% + 6% + 47%) = 85% 
 
And for category 05, the total share is found as: 
 
38% ∗5% + 15% ∗(79% ∗(23% + 8%)) = 6% 
 
Which gives a total allocation for the two relevant categories for the dwelling life cycle: 
 
32% ∗84% + 5% ∗6% = 28% 
 
And considering the household spending allocation for Denmark at 47.5%, the total economic allocation of the 
share of a person equivalent capacity in every impact category assessed is found as: 
 
47.5% ∗28% = 13.3% 
 
A summation of the key numbers for the economic allocation for Denmark is shown in Table 55. 
 
TABLE 55 – ECONOMIC ALLOCATION KEY FIGURES, DENMARK
