Australian allocation 
The Australian allocation key is found by using data from the Australian Bureau of Statistics. A Household 
expenditure survey was likewise found for Australia, however, as the latest survey has not come out with 
detailed expenditure item, the survey overall data from 2015/2016 are used (Australian Bureau of Statistics 
2017b), and then divided by the latest published data for detailed items, published for 2009/2010 in 2012 
(Australian Bureau of Statistics 2011). The data published by the Australian Bureau of Statistics follow a system 
for Household Expenditure Classification (HEC), however, this is closely linked to COICOP (Australian Bureau of 
Statistics 2012). Due to the differences the system/distribution division is a bit different then the Danish, but 
the calculation method is the same. 
 
It is assumed that the HEC categories 01, 02 and 07, contains all relevant expenses considering the 
house/building. As these may contain irrelevant sub-expenses, these are analysed further. Considering the 
sub-categories shown in Table 57, only categories which imply fixed installation or necessary installations have 
been accounted for. Things like e.g. household textiles and glassware are considered movables and not crucial 
for the dwelling. Additionally, services are not included, for which reason category 08 was neglected. The 
categories highlighted are the ones which contribution have been used directly. The ones not highlighted have 
been analysed further.

Denmark is 2.1. However, these numbers cover all types of residential buildings, wherefore the specific 
statistics for the scenarios addressed are investigated, using the statistics BOL103 (Danmarks Statistik 2017). 
For both the reference and the Danish scenario, the output is chosen as listed in Table 62, along with the 
average number of persons living in the household.

Appendix 30 – Validation discussion 
When modelling and setting up the input for the validation scenario, scenario 5, the inputs were based on 
(Brejnrod 2015). However, some information were only explained perfunctory and thus not in detail for 
enabling direct re-modelling. In order to validate the present modelling and determine possible variations, the 
following assumptions were made, based on and to supplement information in (Brejnrod 2015): 
 
- 
The exact maintenance materials are not listed specifically anywhere, thus estimations based on the 
reports mentioning ’components exposed to the ambient air’ being replaced were made to calculate 
maintenance quantities. The following assumptions were made: 
1. Roof tiles (not applied as lifetime is 60 years) 
2. Plaster board (applied 1, lifetime 40) 
3. Paint (applied 4times, life time 10 years) 
4. Window (applied once, life time 25 years) 
5. Carpet (applied once, lifetime 30 years) 
6. Installations 
1. Sanitary ceramics(once, life time 40 years) 
2. Hot water tank (once, life time 30 years) 
3. Circulation pump (twice, life time 20 years) 
4. Ventilation unit (once, life time 25 years) 
5. Heaters (once, life time 30 years) 
- 
The transport processes, distances og specific processes they’re applied on, are not mentioned 
anywhere. Thus it was assumed 30 for production, 20 for EoL. Applied on all datasets 
- 
The construction energy is not explained which source, thus it was assumed to be 100% electricity. 
- 
Densities applied on materials (for conversion of datasets from m3 to kg). 
 
Additionally, to not knowing, thus not modelling, all inputs and details equal, some other variances occur in 
between the model in (Brejnrod 2015) and the present project model. These are some of the natural 
differences occuring when comparing two different-modelled project and these are the variations on which 
the main deviations between results is verified. The variations are found as: 
 
- 
Not all applied processes/flows in from the database are similar, as some datasets may be estimated 
more accurate or suitable, than what was estimated in (Brejnrod 2015). 
- 
The software for processing the models are different. Whereas (Brejnrod 2015) used GaBi, the present 
project has used openLCA. 
- 
The ecoinvent database used in (Brejnrod 2015) is v2.2, whereas the present used in version 3.3. This 
results in some changed datasets and processes, e.g. not all EoL processes used in (Brejnrod 2015) 
where found in the current database. 
- 
The LCIA methodology applied in (Brejnrod 2015) was ReCiPe (knowledge obtained through personal 
communication with Kathrine Brejnrod), whereas this project uses ILCD 2011. 
1. This, for once, incurred a significant difference in that timber e.g. is acocunted for beneficial, 
thus negative impacts, in the impact category climate change’ in the present model, which did 
not occur in (Brejnrod 2015). 
- 
The present model has been grouped according to SfB (HFB 2011) to a further detailed level than 
(Brejnrod 2015), for which reason the component contribution in the present model is less aggregated. 
 
Another uncertainty could occur in considering the distributions of electricity used in Denmark. In (Brejnrod 
2015) it is stated that they distributed 65% to heat and 35% to electricity. According to the reference and thus 
also the applied in the present project, the distribution is oppisite, thus 65% if the energy is electricity and 35% 
for heat. It is not known whether this is a typo in the report, or if the distributions is applied opposite. 
 
==PAGE:181==
 
Appendix 30 – Validation discussion 
 
Page 83 of 136 
 
One of the great variations found between the current model and the one by (Brejnrod 2015), whas the 
component contributions where the current models is dominated by the installations to a great extend. While 
it is somewhat concluded that the current model contains some sort of modelling issue in regards to the 
installations, it is also not known how (Brejnrod 2015) handled the conversion from the dataset unit which is 
given in [pieces], into the input listed as [kg/m2].
